Abstract

Machine learning (ML) technique is a critical tool to promote optimal design and ensure reliable and efficient products and processes in the manufacturing industry, since it can discover hidden knowledge and build complex relationships by learning patterns from data. However, the inherent ‘black-box’ nature of ML presents a major challenge in interpreting the mechanism and outcomes of the models. Moreover, reliable ML predictions are highly dependent on the amount and quality of training data. To address these issues, physics-informed machine learning (PIML), also known as scientific machine learning, has emerged as a new research field. PIML incorporates physical and domain knowledge into ML models to guide the ML training process, which enables more interpretable and reliable models. To fully leverage the advantages of PIML and promote the advancement of design and manufacturing, it is essential for researchers to understand the available PIML methodologies and the technical challenges of PIML methods. This article provides a systematic review of the state-of-the-art in PIML, focusing on the methodologies of integrating physics into ML. The PIML techniques can be grouped into three categories, including hybrid models, physical loss-based models, and physics-embedded architectures. Each of these categories is further stratified according to different integration approaches and ML models. The methods and applications of each technique are summarized. In addition, the technical challenges and potential opportunities of PIML are critically analyzed and discussed, providing a roadmap to narrow the research gaps in PIML.

1 Introduction

Machine learning (ML) has emerged as a widely utilized technique in science and engineering to extract features from data, reveal nonlinear relationships within large datasets, or generate predictive models. When used for the prediction purpose, an ML model is trained based on sampled data, and its structure and parameters are adjusted through iterative processes, without requiring prior knowledge. ML has been extensively applied in design and manufacturing, with various applications such as design optimization, simulation, virtual prototyping, quality control, manufacturing process monitoring, process optimization, process control, robotics, and automation. However, one of the main challenges in design and manufacturing applications is the availability of large training datasets. Complex ML models with a large number of parameters require a significant amount of training data to avoid overfitting. Yet, experimental measurements and simulations in engineering can be very expensive. Another challenge of ML models is their limited extrapolation capability and interpretability in predictions. To improve the accuracy and trustworthiness of ML model predictions, one growing trend is to make “greybox” ML models instead of traditionally “blackbox” ones. When ML models can incorporate prior knowledge of the specific problems, the training efficiency and prediction accuracy can be improved.

In the past three decades, researchers have developed various strategies to embed physical models and domain knowledge into ML models. These strategies are nowadays referred to as 

physics-informed machine learning

 (PIML). There are three general approaches to embed physical models and domain knowledge. The first approach is to construct hybrid models that combine ML with physical models sequentially or in parallel for improved prediction [

1

javascript:;

], or to create physics-informed datasets [

2

javascript:;

]. The second approach is to use physical models as constraints to guide the training process of ML models [

3

javascript:;

–

5

javascript:;

]. The third approach focuses on the development of new ML architectures that leverage problem-specific characteristics and incorporate physics-based guidance to enhance interpretability and ensure physical consistency [

6

javascript:;

–

9

javascript:;

]. In these approaches, physical knowledge has been utilized and integrated in ML models through different forms and representations, such as synthetic data, mathematical models, prior experiences, and physical rules. In the literature, these hybrid approaches of integrating domain knowledge in data-driven modeling are also referred to as 

scientific machine learning

. Figure 

1

javascript:;

 systematically summarizes the constitution of existing PIML techniques (figures of prediction and simulation are adapted from Ref. [

10

javascript:;

]).

Fig. 1

View large

Download slide

Overview of PIML techniques

Fig. 1

View large

Download slide

Overview of PIML techniques

Close modal

The integration of physical knowledge with ML improves the reliability and transparency of the ML models and the accuracy of their predictions. PIML helps reduce the high dependence of ML models on the quality and quantity of input data. However, current PIML techniques are still not capable of accurately modeling and predicting complex problems as traditional physics-based simulations for industry-scale applications. They struggle to solve problems such as modeling the process–structural–property relationship in additive manufacturing, predicting the behavior of materials with phase transitions or nonlinear material responses, and solving multiscale and multiphysics problems [

11

javascript:;

,

12

javascript:;

]. Although physics-based models and knowledge have been embedded in PIML models, they do not have the same extrapolation capability as pure physics-based simulations.

To fully understand the strength and weakness of PIML and properly address its bottleneck issues, a comprehensive overview of this research field can help clarify some misunderstanding and reveal the crucial limitations of PIML techniques. Although several articles have already reviewed the recent development of PIML [

13

javascript:;

–

18

javascript:;

], they focused on the potential applications of PIML without revealing the key challenges of the PIML methodologies. Good strategies for overcoming these challenges are needed in the research community. Furthermore, most articles devote the same attention to all PIML techniques, without considering the nuances of their impacts on the interpretability of ML models. The goals of this article are to highlight the challenges of PIML by providing a complete historical view, summarizing the methodologies developed in conjunction with different ML approaches, and identifying the common limitations, as well as to offer some prospects of future research opportunities.

The typical physics integration methods and their related advanced techniques in the three categories of PIML methods—namely, hybrid models, physical loss-based models, and physics-embedded architectures—are expounded separately. The advantages and disadvantages of each PIML technique are also examined, accompanied by examples of their applications. By further revealing the existing challenges and potential opportunities of PIML, this article establishes a foundation for researchers to better explore, utilize, and develop PIML techniques. The remainder of this article is organized as follows. Section 

2

https://asmedigitalcollection.asme.org#s2

 summarizes the development and applications of hybrid model techniques. Section 

3

https://asmedigitalcollection.asme.org#s3

 reviews the methods and physical sources of constructing a physics-based loss function. The advanced techniques developed to improve the performance of physics-loss-based models are also introduced. The details of physics-embedded architectures, including typical model-specified techniques and generic techniques for neural networks, are comprehensively presented in Section 

4

https://asmedigitalcollection.asme.org#s4

. The challenges and potential opportunities for PIML are highlighted in Section 

5

https://asmedigitalcollection.asme.org#s5

. Section 

6

https://asmedigitalcollection.asme.org#s6

 gives the concluding remarks.

2 Hybrid Models

Constructing hybrid data-driven physics-based models is the simplest way to introduce physics into ML. Some hybrid models were already developed in the 1990s to apply ML to solve design and manufacturing problems. This technique involves integrating physical models with ML models in parallel or serial to realize complementary strengths. In other words, the physical model and the ML model operate as independent modules.

2.1 Residual Modeling.

The first technique in hybrid models is residual modeling, where ML models are trained to predict errors or residuals of physical models so that the prediction accuracy of physical models can be further improved [

1

javascript:;

]. That is, when the physical model  y ^ = f ( x )  predicts some quantity  y ⁠ , the prediction error  ϵ ( x )  also depends on input  x ⁠ , where  y = y ^ + ϵ ( x ) ⁠ . The ML serves as the surrogate model of  ϵ ( x ) ⁠ , as illustrated in Fig. 

2

javascript:;

.

Fig. 2

View large

Download slide

Residual modeling

Fig. 2

View large

Download slide

Residual modeling

Close modal

The ML models adopted in residual modeling are usually some linear regression models. Residual modeling has been widely applied to correct the predictions produced by reduced-order models (ROMs) in dynamical systems and some governing equations. ROMs refer to physical models with reduced complexity compared to the real-world design, and their performance is significantly compromised due to truncation. Guo and Hesthaven [

19

javascript:;

] adopted residual modeling to approximate parametrized time-dependent problems. They utilized proper orthogonal decomposition (POD) to construct the reduced basis of full-order solutions, and modeled error surrogates using Gaussian process regression (GPR). In model order reduction of thermal fluids, San and Maulik [

20

javascript:;

] trained a feed-forward neural network (FNN) to account for residual effects of using POD bases to discard Bousinessq equations. The proposed method achieved excellent performance in effectively stabilizing temporal mode evolution. In turbulence modeling, Wang et al. [

21

javascript:;

] employed random forest to model the errors in Reynolds stresses predicted by Reynolds-averaged Navier–Stokes (RANS) equations. The prediction errors were modeled as functions of mean flow features derived from the RANS results.

2.2 Intermediate Quantity.

ML can also be applied to construct intermediate variables or inner components of physical models. That is, the ML model  x = F ( z )  is constructed to predict the physical quantity  x  from the observable  z ⁠ . The predicted  x  is then used as the input of the physical model  f ( x ) ⁠ , as illustrated in Fig. 

3

javascript:;

.

Fig. 3

View large

Download slide

Intermediate quantity

Fig. 3

View large

Download slide

Intermediate quantity

Close modal

Intermediate quantity technique contributes to improving the representation and computational accuracy as well as the flexibility of physical models in analyzing complex systems. For example, Parish and Duraisamy [

22

javascript:;

] adopted GPR to construct the variables representing deficient physics in the turbulence closure model. This method improved the predictive capability of RANS solvers in predicting turbulent channel flow. In geodynamics modeling, Goldstein et al. [

23

javascript:;

] integrated a predictor derived from genetic programming for oscillatory flow ripples into a morphodynamic model of inner-shelf sorted bedforms. The developed hybrid model successfully achieved parameterizations and captured two pattern modes of sorted bedforms that were previously absent from the model. Similarly, artificial neural networks (ANNs) were used to fit component functions in a high-dimensional model representation to build multidimensional potentials [

24

javascript:;

]. The neural network procedure not only enables the merger of modal terms to reduce computational costs but also facilitates the determination of high-order modal terms, thereby improving the quality of potential representation. This method achieves a root mean square error of less than 10  cm − 1  for fitting and an error of within 1  cm − 1  for the calculation of the energy level of the hydrogen peroxide surface.

2.3 Physics-Based Feature Engineering.

Physics-based feature engineering is the third technique in hybrid models, which aims to inform the input of ML with physical meaning. It is also the most common technique in hybrid models. Physics-based feature engineering uses the outputs of physical models as the inputs of ML models to predict high-fidelity quantities [

2

javascript:;

], similar to multifidelity modeling. Specifically, ML model  F  can be constructed as  y = F ( x , f ( x ) )  with both  x  and  y ^  as inputs to predict the physical quantity  y ⁠ , as shown in Fig. 

4

javascript:;

. Physics-based feature engineering helps to promote ML model’s exploration of deep physical relationships with limited experimental data. Thus, this technique is an alternative way to address the difficulty of acquiring large amounts of specified data needed for ML through experiments.

Fig. 4

View large

Download slide

Physics-based feature engineering

Fig. 4

View large

Download slide

Physics-based feature engineering

Close modal

Physics-based feature engineering can be achieved through two approaches: physical feature augmentation and physics-based preprocessing. The simplest way to achieve physical feature augmentation is to use simulation models to generate an ML training dataset [

25

javascript:;

–

27

javascript:;

]. These simulation models are typically constructed based on governing equations or system models, enabling the generation of synthetic data aligning with the underlying physics. Finite element models (FEMs) are commonly used for generating physical data in applications involving solid structures, where the system’s physics is directly incorporated through mathematical equations [

28

javascript:;

]. For example, in metal additive manufacturing, FEM was used to generate geometrical features of the melt pool, enabling the improved visual geometry group to construct process–property relationships [

29

javascript:;

]. In structural health monitoring, FEM was adopted to generate structural data of steel beams for training a convolutional neural network (CNN) [

30

javascript:;

] and to form fault vibration signals of gearboxes for input into an extreme learning machine [

31

javascript:;

]. Except for FEM-related works, other typical simulation models that have been used to generate physical synthetic data include the phase field model, the fast Fourier transform-based crystal plasticity model [

32

javascript:;

], the pseudo-two-dimensional model [

33

javascript:;

], and the diode model [

34

javascript:;

]. Tian et al. [

29

javascript:;

] proved that simulation-based feature augmentation can improve the prediction accuracy of ML compared with pure data-driven ML models. However, to ensure reliability of the obtained physical features, the constructed simulation models should be high-fidelity, which imposes stringent requirements on researchers’ expertise.

Physical features can also be directly generated through some empirical models and theoretical equations. For instance, Hao et al. [

35

javascript:;

] employed the Basquin model of the S–N curve, Neuber’s rule, and the Smith–Watson–Topper model to generate the reference life of unnotched specimens, the maximum stress at the notch root, and an energy-type damage parameter for evaluating notch fatigue in aerospace alloys. A prediction accuracy of more than 97% and the improved generalization ability of adopted traditional ML classifiers were achieved. Similarly, Wang et al. [

36

javascript:;

] applied Paris’ law to generate the fatigue crack growth life of materials in additive manufacturing to predict fatigue life, which led to improved fatigue extrapolation and robustness of ANN and support vector regression.

Physics-based preprocessing refers to applying some physical laws or principles to process raw data or extract features that are most pertinent and significant to the problem. One typical approach for the former is to employ some transformations constructed from physical principles or theory equations to map the raw data into physically meaningful features. As described by Xie et al. [

37

javascript:;

], the wavelet transform was applied to convert infrared thermal histories into frequency-time plots. The transformed features improved the predictive capability of the CNN for location-dependent mechanical properties of additively manufactured parts, even when using limited and noisy experimental data. Other transformation models reported for physics-based preprocessing include logarithmic transformations [

38

javascript:;

] and the Fourier transform [

39

javascript:;

]. For the work of physics-based feature extraction, Tian et al. [

40

javascript:;

] developed a method based on spectral kurtosis and cross-correlation to extract fault features from vibration signals of motor bearings. The cross-correlation model was used to select subsignals containing the fault characteristic frequency, while the spectral kurtosis was used to generate fault features from these subsignals. This strategy enabled the detection of intermittent and hidden faults in motor bearings using K-nearest neighbor distance analysis. The outline of physics-based feature engineering methods can be summarized in Fig. 

5

javascript:;

.

Fig. 5

View large

Download slide

Outline of physics-based feature engineering methods

Fig. 5

View large

Download slide

Outline of physics-based feature engineering methods

Close modal

3 Physics-Based Loss Functions

Unlike the hybrid model techniques in which physics has no effect on ML’s training process, physics-based losses are to enhance the training, tuning, and evaluation of ML with physics. Physical knowledge, such as governing equations and mathematical relationships, is incorporated into the loss function of ML models as the physical loss. Through backpropagation updates, the physical loss can guide ML models to capture the patterns that align with the defined physical laws. The idea of formulating physics-based loss to train ML models can be traced back more than two decades ago. In 1998, Lagaris et al. [

3

javascript:;

] proposed to use an ANN to solve differential equations  y = f ( x , ψ ( x ) , ∇ ψ ( x ) , ∇ 2 ψ ( x ) )  by formulating the loss function of neural networks as L P = ‖ y − f ( x , ψ ( x ) , ∇ ψ ( x ) , ∇ 2 ψ ( x ) ) ‖ 2 (1) where  y  is the output of neural networks,  f ( ⋅ )  is a general operator of differential equations, and  ψ ( x )  is the solution to be computed. Lagaris et al. [

3

javascript:;

] demonstrated this approach with several ordinary differential equations (ODEs) and partial differential equations (PDEs). In 2007, Cursi and Koscianski [

4

javascript:;

] extended the approach by combining data loss  L D  and physical loss  L P  with the Lagrange multiplier  λ  as L = ( 1 − λ ) L D + λ L P (2)  

That is, the physical loss is the regularization and serves as a soft constraint to guide the training of neural networks.

This concept of physics-based loss function has recently been revived and has gained much more attention. One of the most popular methods is a physics-informed neural network (PINN) [

5

javascript:;

], also known as a physics-constrained neural network (PCNN). When the physics-based loss function approaches zero, it indicates that the predictions satisfy both the physical laws and the labeled data simultaneously [

41

javascript:;

]. Physical knowledge is thereby integrated into the model [

42

javascript:;

]. These physical laws range from simple formulas to complex PDEs [

13

javascript:;

,

43

javascript:;

]. The following sections will detail the different methods of constructing physical loss and the related techniques for improving the performance of physics-loss-based PIML models.

3.1 Loss Function by Automatic Differentiation.

Neural networks are designed to use spatiotemporal coordinates as inputs and produce variables of interest as outputs [

44

javascript:;

]. This process generates a computational graph that maps the inputs to the outputs. During backpropagation, the gradients for each node within the graph must be computed [

45

javascript:;

]. Automatic differentiation (AD) technique applies the chain rule to evaluate derivatives of arbitrary orders, thereby obtaining the temporal and spatial derivatives of the variables of interest [

46

javascript:;

], as shown in Fig. 

6

javascript:;

. These derivatives are subsequently used to construct the physical loss functions in accordance with the target PDEs.

Fig. 6

View large

Download slide

Schematic diagram of AD including forward accumulations and gradient computations

Fig. 6

View large

Download slide

Schematic diagram of AD including forward accumulations and gradient computations

Close modal

Since derivatives at any point within a domain can be easily obtained by AD, this type of loss function can be readily adapted to various problems [

47

javascript:;

,

48

javascript:;

], whether they involve regular or irregular geometries [

49

javascript:;

–

51

javascript:;

]. In heat analysis, such loss functions can address both forward and inverse problems to obtain the temperature distributions and predict thermal properties [

44

javascript:;

,

52

javascript:;

]. In fluid dynamics, they help predict flow fields or reconstruct missing information with available measurements [

53

javascript:;

]. Furthermore, in multiphysics modeling, thermofluid dynamics in the melt pool of metal additive manufacturing can be monitored using AD-based PINNs [

54

javascript:;

]. Conservation laws of momentum, mass, and energy during the additive manufacturing process were used to model the three-dimensional (3D) melt pool [

55

javascript:;

]. The prediction discrepancy (with respect to the experimental measurements) of PINN was decreased by 14.2% compared to numerical simulations, even with a moderate amount of labeled datasets. In addition, they have been applied to analyze interactions between blood flow and vessel wall mechanics [

49

javascript:;

,

56

javascript:;

]. Arzani et al. [

57

javascript:;

] developed a PINN-based framework to accurately quantify near-wall blood flow and wall shear stress in diseased arterial flows using sparse measurements and partial boundary conditions. Figure 

7

javascript:;

 illustrates the framework of AD-based PINNs with alternative neural network models.

3.2 Loss Function by Numerical Discretization.

In addition to AD, physical loss functions can also be constructed based on numerical discretization of PDEs [

58

javascript:;

]. Common numerical methods include the finite difference method (FDM) [

59

javascript:;

], the finite volume method (FVM) [

60

javascript:;

], and the FEM [

61

javascript:;

]. This approach requires neural networks to generate predictions on a predefined grid, ensuring compatibility with the discrete domain representation [

62

javascript:;

]. By leveraging these numerical techniques, the outputs from neural networks are directly used to construct the various derivatives in the target PDEs, which avoids the lengthy chain of AD. The framework of PINNs incorporated with numerical methods is shown in Fig. 

8

javascript:;

.

Close modal

Recently, a physics-informed fully convolutional network (FCN) was developed by employing the FDM to construct a physical loss function for a parametric heat conduction equation [

63

javascript:;

]. This model can simultaneously predict the temperature field and estimate unknown properties. The results for forward temperature prediction and inverse estimation of the unknown thermal diffusivity are shown in Fig. 

9

javascript:;

. The maximum error of the predicted temperature is  1.47 × 10 − 2 ⁠ , and the accuracy of the estimated thermal diffusivity is 99.3%. Chiu et al. [

64

javascript:;

] compared their proposed hybrid network with numerical differentiation to AD-based PINNs and found that its accuracy exceeded that of the traditional one in various problems. Ranade et al. [

65

javascript:;

] proposed a ML-based solver for the Navier–Stokes equation using FVM. The experiments showed that the computation accuracy, stability, and convergence speed of the proposed solver are significantly improved compared to the ANSYS Fluent   solver. Additionally, Zhu et al. [

66

javascript:;

] developed a finite volume physics-informed U-net (FV-PIUnet) by integrating FVM into the U-net and applied this approach to resolve Burgers’ equation. The results in Fig. 

10

javascript:;

 illustrate that FV-PIUnet achieves higher accuracy compared to an AD-based PINN, with a maximum error of 0.37 for FV-PIUnet and 1.76 for the AD-based PINN. Yadav and Ganesan [

67

javascript:;

] combined FEM and a neural network to solve a two-dimensional (2D) convective-diffusion equation. Their approach suppressed the undershoots and overshoots that were encountered in the existing neural network-based PDE solvers. These studies demonstrated that the significant drawbacks of AD in constructing physical loss can be effectively alleviated.

Combining PINNs with numerical loss functions can be used as surrogate models for traditional numerical approaches to solve linear and nonlinear PDEs [

65

javascript:;

,

68

javascript:;

]. By training with initial conditions or early stage solutions, the surrogate model can effectively extrapolate the solution over the untrained period [

69

javascript:;

,

70

javascript:;

]. This approach proves particularly advantageous in addressing high-dimensional stochastic PDEs, such as solving fluid flow through random heterogeneous media. Zhu et al. [

71

javascript:;

] proposed a physics-constrained surrogate model for the Darcy flow problem. Their model does not require labeled data and achieves results comparable to numerical simulations. In such cases, the surrogate model is capable of efficiently handling and solving the equations.

3.3 Additional Regularization Term.

Although integrating loss terms based on governing PDEs into the total loss function facilitates the neural network to learn physical laws, there are still challenges in prediction accuracy and convergence. Consequently, some studies have introduced additional loss terms, also known as regularization terms, to address these issues [

72

javascript:;

,

73

javascript:;

].

The regularization terms include two categories. The first one involves regularizing the individual weights when the loss terms are combined by weighting. That is, an additional regularization term is added in the loss function to constrain the magnitudes of the individual weights to prevent any negative impact on individual losses [

74

javascript:;

]. The second category is to construct regularization terms using additional physical information or constraints. For example, when solving inviscid Burgers and Euler equations in compressible flow problems, introducing artificial viscosity in the original PDEs facilitates faster convergence [

75

javascript:;

], which can be expressed as L av = 1 n ∑ i = 1 n ( ∂ t u ^ i + ∇ f ( u ^ i ) − μ ^ i ∇ 2 u ^ i ) 2 (3) where  u ^  is the output of the neural networks,  f  is the flux function, and  μ ^  represents the artificial viscosity coefficient. In solving heat transfer problems, the incorporation of entropy inequalities and enthalpy conservation relations can further enhance the neural network’s ability to learn physical laws [

76

javascript:;

]. The loss function for entropy inequality is given by L ent = ∑ i = 1 N ( max ( 0 , ∫ q ^ i ⋅ d A ) ) 2 (4) where  q ^  denotes the entropy flux, and  A  represents the differential area of the grid cell. Moreover, when constructing physical loss functions using numerical methods, additional numerical scheme constraints can be introduced as the regularization term. For instance, Patel et al. [

76

javascript:;

] utilized total variation diminishing (TVD) loss  L TVD  to ensure that new oscillations or spurious variations are not introduced into the predicted solutions, as L TVD = ∑ n max ( 0 , T V h ( u n + 1 ) − T V h ( u n ) ) 2 (5) where the discretized form of  T V h  is given by T V h ( u n ) = ∑ i | u i + 1 , n − u i , n | (6)  

Other examples of regularization have also been developed. For instance, the regularization based on homotopy continuation allows for tracing the observations and identifying multiple solutions of PDEs [

77

javascript:;

]. Grouping losses locally in different regions can alleviate the challenge of magnitude difference [

78

javascript:;

]. A regularization that penalizes the unstable solutions based on the spectrum of the Jacobian matrix can avoid unstable fixed points in dynamical problems [

79

javascript:;

].

3.4 Weighting Strategy.

Training PINNs is a multitask learning process because the total loss function of a PINN often involves the components of sampling data loss, initial and boundary condition (I/BC) loss, and PDEs loss. All of the sublosses are expected to converge simultaneously. However, significant differences between the magnitudes of these losses hinder the optimizer to converge all loss terms [

80

javascript:;

]. Therefore, it is necessary to combine them into a total loss function using a weighted approach. To rationalize the contributions of all the loss terms, various weighting strategies have been developed to appropriately adjust the weights. Liu and Wang [

11

javascript:;

] first proposed an adaptive weighting scheme to adaptively adjust the weights of PINN. For each epoch of training, the weights are adjusted based on the respective magnitudes of physical losses. The effectiveness of this approach was demonstrated in solving multiphysics problems such as heat transfer and the Allen-Cahn equation in dendritic growth. Another self-adaptive loss balanced method is based on the maximum likelihood estimation which was proposed by Xiang et al. [

81

javascript:;

]. With this loss-balance strategy, the robustness of PINN is improved against variations in initial weights, the number of observations, hidden layers, and neurons per layer. However, in most PINN-based research, the weights of losses are only manually tuned. For example, Wight and Zhao [

82

javascript:;

] applied a large fixed weight for the loss of initial conditions to improve overall accuracy when solving the Allen-Cahn and Cahn-Hilliard equations. The manually tuned weights rely entirely on experience and can vary from case to case.

In the adaptive weighting scheme, the total loss function for model training is defined as L ( w ) = λ D L D ( w ) + λ P L P ( w ) + λ I L I ( w ) + λ S L S ( w ) (7) where  L I  and  L S  represent the losses arising from violations of the initial and boundary conditions, respectively. The weights of different losses  λ D ⁠ ,  λ P ⁠ ,  λ I ⁠ , and  λ S  satisfy the constraint  λ D + λ P + λ I + λ S = 1 ⁠ .   The adaptive scheme involves assigning weights to the different losses, such as λ i = L i L D + L P + L I + L S , i ∈ { D , P , I , S } (8) for each iteration of the training process. In other words, the weights are assigned in proportion to the respective individual losses.   To further improve the convergence, Liu and Wang [

83

javascript:;

] further proposed a new minimax architecture for PINN with accelerated weight adjustment. The training of PINN becomes solving minimax problems. The optimization objective is given by min w max ℏ L ( w , ℏ ) = λ D ( ℏ ) L D ( w ) + λ P ( ℏ ) L P ( w ) + λ I ( ℏ ) L I ( w ) + λ S ( ℏ ) L S ( w ) (9) where the weights of different losses  λ D ⁠ ,  λ P ⁠ ,  λ I ⁠ , and  λ S  are now functions of parameters  ℏ = ( ℏ D , ℏ P , ℏ I , ℏ S ) ⁠ . Training is to minimize the possible loss for a worst-case (maximum loss) scenario. That is, the total loss  L ( w , ℏ )  is maximized in the parameter subspace of  ℏ  while minimized in the parameter subspace of  w ⁠ . During the training, the weights of different losses  λ ’s are adjusted to maximize the total loss  L ( w , ℏ )  in  ℏ  subspace, whereas the weights of the neural network  w ’s are tuned to minimize the total loss  L ( w , ℏ ) ⁠ . The weights of different losses can be defined as the softmax function λ i ( ℏ ) = exp ( ℏ i ) exp ( ℏ D ) + exp ( ℏ P ) + exp ( ℏ I ) + exp ( ℏ S ) , i ∈ { D , P , I , S } (10)  

The applications in solving a heat transfer problem demonstrated that the computational efficiency of the minimax architecture is higher than that of the gradient descent method in traditional PINNs.

3.5 Gradient Improvement Method.

When training PINNs/PCNNs to solve PDEs, many factors related to gradient computation may lead to poor performance. Common factors include gradient imbalance [

84

javascript:;

], gradient pathology [

85

javascript:;

], multitask conflicts [

86

javascript:;

], and uneven gradient distribution caused by multiscale problems [

13

javascript:;

]. The gradient improvement method helps alleviate these problems by improving the gradient calculation process, such as optimizing the direction or path of the gradient update [

87

javascript:;

].

Researchers have developed many gradient improvement methods. Gradient balancing is one of the most representative approaches, which aims at addressing gradient imbalance problems among multiple loss terms. It can be achieved by dynamically adjusting the weights based on the gradient norm [

84

javascript:;

]. The principle of this method is formulated as λ i ( t + 1 ) = | ∇ θ L | ¯ max ( | ∇ θ L i ( t ) | , ϱ ) (11) where  λ i ( t + 1 )  denotes the weight of the  i th loss term at the  t + 1  iteration.  | ∇ θ L | ¯  refers to the mean absolute value of the gradients of all the loss terms.  θ  is the network parameters.  ϱ  is a small positive value to avoid a zero denominator. Gradient balancing can also be achieved through gradient normalization, which involves rescaling the gradients of each loss term to ensure consistent magnitudes across tasks [

85

javascript:;

]. Another effective gradient balancing method is task prioritization [

13

javascript:;

], which balances the gradient by adjusting the optimization steps to compensate for the gradient difference. Specifically, it uses an adaptive sampling strategy to increase the sample points in regions where the gradients are smaller, such as when the PDE residual is higher.  

The second method to improve gradient propagation is the optimization of the gradient path. It involves constraining the gradient path or using advanced optimizers to improve optimization trajectories, thereby avoiding local optima and slow convergence [

84

javascript:;

]. Gradient clipping and gradient projection are common methods for constraining the gradient path. Specifically, gradient clipping stabilizes the optimization process by limiting the maximum norm of gradients to avoid the gradient explosion issue. Gradient projection projects the gradients into directions that satisfy the targeted physical or mathematical constraints. This ensures that the parameter update process satisfies the defined constraints. Thus, the invalid gradient update can be avoided [

86

javascript:;

]. In the aspect of employing advanced optimizers, Adam with decoupled weight decay (AdamW) [

88

javascript:;

] and limited-memory Broyden Fletcher Goldfarb Shanno (L-BFGS) [

89

javascript:;

] are the commonly used methods. The AdamW optimizer reduces the oscillation of the gradient path by introducing weight decay regularization parameters. The L-BFGS uses second-order information (Hessian approximation) to calculate a better descent direction [

13

javascript:;

].

Gradient regularization is the third technique that can avoid gradient pathology and improve gradient stability. The related techniques include gradient norm regularization and high-order gradient regularization. The former functions by penalizing large gradients, while the latter aims to smooth the high-order derivatives of the network output [

13

javascript:;

,

84

javascript:;

]. Complementary to regularization, gradient decomposition techniques address the issue of conflicting gradients in multitask optimization. For instance, projecting conflicting gradients (PCGrad ) [

90

javascript:;

] resolves gradient conflicts by projecting the gradient of one task onto the normal plane of another. Thus, it can eliminate incompatible components and enable more consistent optimization [

84

javascript:;

]. In terms of gradient computation, the adjoint method [

91

javascript:;

] improves efficiency by decomposing the total gradient into a forward pass and an adjoint (backward) pass. This method avoids direct computation of all intermediate derivatives, allowing scalable and memory-efficient training, which is especially useful when computing high-order derivatives in PINNs.

3.6 Sampling Strategies for Residual Point.

Sampling residual points in the computational domain is critical for constructing AD-based physical loss functions, as it directly affects convergence behavior, solution accuracy, and computational cost. Conventional strategies, such as random sampling (e.g., Latin hypercube sampling) and uniform sampling, may lead to large local errors in strongly nonlinear regions or where gradients vary rapidly. Consequently, various adaptive sampling strategies have been proposed. An intuitive approach is to repeatedly sample according to the error distribution. Specifically, residual points are first randomly selected and used to train PINNs, and their locations are then adjusted according to the predictions of the model. This process can be repeated multiple times to obtain a more refined set of residual points. For instance, Cen and Zou [

92

javascript:;

] conducted 10 rounds of resampling, resulting in a substantially lower  L 2  relative error compared to models trained without this technique. However, the training time increased nearly fivefold.

Another approach for distributing residual points is to leverage the patterns of boundary or initial conditions, which can significantly improve the prediction accuracy. Since boundary and initial conditions have a substantial impact on subsequent solutions, increasing sampling density in these regions is highly beneficial. For instance, Zhao et al. [

93

javascript:;

] sampled residual points based on the initial condition when solving one-dimensional (1D) and 2D Euler equations. Their results demonstrated accurate predictions of both the initial and final states. The gradients at the interface are also effectively captured. In addition, the nature of the governing PDEs can guide the placement of residual points. In hyperbolic PDEs, shock formation is closely related to the intersection of characteristic lines, making it helpful to add more sampling points around them to ensure accurate shock prediction. Mao et al. [

50

javascript:;

] employed a uniform distribution of residual points and increased sampling density along characteristic lines, yielding a sharper shock wave. Similarly, Jagtap et al. [

94

javascript:;

] precomputed the positions of forward and rearward Mach lines, and then added more sampling points near these lines in an otherwise randomly distributed set, leading to improved shock predictions. Compared with traditional methods, both of these strategies necessitate additional prior knowledge, which will pose challenges in high-dimensional or complex problems.

3.7 Advanced Architecture-Based PINN.

Traditional PINNs are primarily built upon multilayer perceptrons (MLPs), leading to low computational efficiency. To address this issue, advanced architecture-based PINN techniques have been developed, which aim to replace MLPs with more advanced architectures. For example, fully connected layers can be changed into convolutional layers to reduce connection densities. Some studies have applied FCNs and U-Net architectures to solve problems related to fluid flow and heat transfer, yielding shorter training times and higher accuracy compared to standard PINNs [

58

javascript:;

,

64

javascript:;

,

95

javascript:;

]. A variational autoencoder (VAE) has been integrated to represent complex geometries of the domain [

96

javascript:;

]. Moreover, in certain surrogate models for PDE solvers, recurrent neural network (RNN) and long-short term memory (LSTM) structures have also been explored, underscoring the potential for even broader design choices in the development of enhanced PINN frameworks [

97

javascript:;

,

98

javascript:;

]. Tong et al. [

70

javascript:;

] presented a novel network inspired by the classical Roe solver to predict the solutions of hyperbolic PDEs. Comparisons with the original solver revealed that the neural network-based approach exhibited slight superiority in shockwave shape and position. Ren et al. [

68

javascript:;

] developed a physics-informed convolutional LSTM to solve the nonlinear convective PDEs. Their method outperformed the original one in terms of solution accuracy, extrapolability, and generalizability. Peng et al. [

99

javascript:;

] developed a graph neural network (GNN) that served as a prediction module. They extended the geometric adaptability to irregular domains by employing unstructured meshes. The predicted velocity fields of fluid flow around a cylinder using the proposed model closely aligned with the ground truth, even with very limited training data. Table 

1

javascript:;

 summarizes the typical literature on advanced architecture-based PINN, with an emphasis on their application cases, merits, and limitations.

3.8 Multifidelity PINN.

Although PINNs require less training data compared to pure data-driven neural networks, their accurate predictions still rely on the input of sufficient high-fidelity data [

74

javascript:;

]. However, high-fidelity data usually imply increased experimental complexity and acquisition challenges, which may not be available in all scenarios. The multifidelity PINN technique was specially developed to reduce the dependence of traditional PINNs on large amounts of high-fidelity data while ensuring high computational accuracy.

The principle of multifidelity PINN is to simultaneously sample data with various fidelity levels during training, which is similar to the concept of multifidelity surrogate modeling [

108

javascript:;

–

110

javascript:;

]. One way to achieve this is to integrate multiple neural networks of different fidelity, which is also referred to as a multifidelity physics-constrained neural network with a minimax architecture (MF-PCNN-MM) [

74

javascript:;

]. The MF-PCNN-MM is able to improve prediction accuracy and training stability by physical constraints and minimax optimization, while also lowering the costs associated with high-fidelity data by utilizing low-fidelity data. Figure 

11

javascript:;

 shows the architecture of MF-PCNN-MM, which consists of two subnetworks.

Fig. 11

View large

Download slide

Framework of MF-PCNN-MM architecture [

74

javascript:;

]. Reprinted with permission from Liu et al. © 2023 ASME.

Fig. 11

View large

Download slide

Framework of MF-PCNN-MM architecture [

74

javascript:;

]. Reprinted with permission from Liu et al. © 2023 ASME.

Close modal

javascript:;

The first subnetwork makes predictions based on low-fidelity data, and its output  U L ( x )  is used as the partial input of the second subnetwork. The second subnetwork  U H ( x , U L )  approximates the correlation function between the low-fidelity and high-fidelity data, which can be expressed as [

108

javascript:;

] u H ( x , u L ) = ρ s ( x ) u L + δ ( x ) (12) where  x  is the input vector.  u L  and  u H  are the predictions of the low-fidelity model and the high-fidelity model, respectively.  ρ s ( x )  represents the multiplicative correlation surrogate, and  δ ( x )  is the additive correlation surrogate. That is, the high-fidelity network  U H ( x , U L )  serves as a surrogate model for the left side of Eq. 

(12)

javascript:;

. The training process of the multifidelity physics-constrained neural network (MF-PCNN) is formulated as a minimax optimization problem using the method in Eq. 

(9)

javascript:;

.  

During the training of MF-PCNN-MM, a bilevel optimization is performed: the loss weights  λ i ( ℏ )  are optimized to maximize the total loss  L ( w , ℏ ) ⁠ , while the neural network parameters  w  are updated to minimize it. This optimization process is equivalent to finding a saddle point in a high-dimensional energy landscape  E ⁠ , where the Dual-Dimer method [

83

javascript:;

] is used to locate such high-order saddle points.

The proposed scheme is demonstrated with three examples: heat equation, Allen-Cahn equation, and dendritic growth during solidification, which involves coupled heat transfer and phase transition. Across all cases, the proposed scheme consistently achieves higher predictive accuracy compared to the multifidelity neural network with minimax architecture (MF-NN-MM). Moreover, it is able to capture more intricate interfacial morphologies and thermal field evolution of the physical systems.

3.9 Physics-Informed Bayesian Neural Networks.

Similar to neural networks, PINNs face the accuracy challenge. Based on the sources of uncertainty, the errors can be categorized as either model-form uncertainty or parameter uncertainty. The model-form uncertainty in neural networks is associated with the choices of network architecture, neuron connections, and activation functions. The choices based on analysts’ preferences and own knowledge naturally introduce bias and potential errors into the model predictions. The parameter uncertainty is associated with the training of neural networks, where errors could be caused by the quality and quantity of training data, as well as training algorithms and training time. Training neural networks with a large number of parameters is performing optimization in the high-dimensional parameter space, and it is very difficult to find the global optimum. Suboptimal parameters will lead to errors in the model predictions. For PINNs, model-form and parameter uncertainty are also associated with the physical models. Simplification, approximation, and truncation of mathematical forms involved in physical models lead to model-form uncertainty, whereas the calibration process is the major source of parameter uncertainty. Therefore, the physical models themselves are additional sources of errors.

Several approaches have been developed to quantify the uncertainty in neural networks, including Bayesian neural networks (network parameters follow statistical distributions), Monte Carlo dropout (neuron connections are randomly disconnected), a hybrid neural network Gaussian process model (the output layer of neural networks is replaced with Gaussian process models), and a neural network ensemble (an ensemble of networks that predict variations of quantities) [

111

javascript:;

]. To quantify the uncertainty in PINNs, physics-constrained Bayesian neural networks (PCBNNs) [

112

javascript:;

,

113

javascript:;

] have been proposed. The weights in PCBNNs are probabilistically distributed, instead of deterministic values as in classical PINNs. The training of PCBNN is based on Bayes’ rule. The goal is to minimize the difference between the distribution observed in training data and the distribution predicted by PCBNN. In the iterative training process, the prior distribution of neural network weights is updated to the posterior given the likelihood of observing the data. The most generic training algorithm is the Markov chain Monte Carlo (MCMC), where the posterior distribution is obtained by sampling. However, training based on MCMC is computationally very expensive. A more efficient alternative is variational inference. In variational inference, the weights are regarded as the latent variables.

Instead of training and updating the weights of PCBNN, recently a strategy of Monte Carlo dropout was developed. In Monte Carlo dropout, weights in traditional neural networks are randomly chosen and set to zero. As a result, the predictions from the ensemble of neural networks are stochastic. The dropout strategy was originally designed for the sensitivity analysis of neuron connectivities. For uncertainty quantification, Monte Carlo dropout provides an efficient way to assess the effect of model-form uncertainty on neural networks.

4 Physics-Embedded Architectures

Despite the physics-based loss function and hybrid model techniques that enable the search space, input, and output data to be physically meaningful, the ML architecture remains a black-box. To force the black-box to be interpretable and further improve ML performance, researchers have devoted significant efforts to constructing physics-embedded architectures. Due to significant differences in theoretical foundations and architectures among different ML models, the methods for embedding physical knowledge vary. In some early work of PIML, specialized ML architecture designs were developed so that physics information is embedded in interpretable ML structures. For instance, Ramuhalli et al. [

6

javascript:;

] designed a finite element neural network, where the stiffness matrix elements are directly mapped to the activation functions of neurons so that the influence of material properties can be predicted. Behler and Parrinello [

7

javascript:;

] constructed neural networks to predict the atomic potential energy, where the activation functions are the radial basis functions that preserve the angular and distance symmetries in atomic systems.

In this section, a detailed overview of the methodologies and applications of several specific physics-embedded ML architectures is provided. Some ML techniques other than neural networks, such as dictionary learning, are also summarized.

4.1 PDE- and Hard-Constrained Neural Network.

Unlike soft-constrained approaches that incorporate PDE residuals into the loss function, hard-constrained neural networks directly enforce specific PDE conditions, such as boundary conditions, initial conditions, or conservation laws, through deliberate network architecture design. This strategy minimizes dependence on residual-based penalties, thereby reducing computational costs and enhancing efficiency in solving PDEs. The very early work of PIML by Lagaris et al. [

3

javascript:;

] explicitly considered the initial and boundary conditions in an analytical form instead of including them in neural networks.

More recently, several approaches were developed to impose hard constraints on the neural network framework, which focus on improving the consistency of predictions with respect to the known conditions. The initial and boundary conditions enforced neural network [

114

javascript:;

–

116

javascript:;

] is a specifically designed hard-constrained framework that embeds boundary or initial conditions directly into the neural network architecture through mathematical construction. For example, Straub et al. [

117

javascript:;

] presented a method to enforce Neumann boundary conditions in PINNs using Fourier feature embeddings. They employed a cosine-only Fourier feature embedding, which ensures that the spatial derivatives vanish at the boundary, thereby hard-enforcing the Neumann condition. Deguchi and Asai [

118

javascript:;

] showed that boundary conditions can be enforced exactly by employing distance functions, in particular normalized R-functions, which convert the PINN output into a boundary-satisfying trial solution. By doing so, it ensures that the network output strictly satisfies the prescribed constraints along the initial or boundary nodes. A typical embedding form can be expressed as y ( x , t ) = g ( x , t ) + h ( x , t ) ⋅ N θ ( x , t ) (13) where  g ( x , t )  is a predefined function that satisfies the initial or boundary conditions.  N θ ( x , t )  is the output of the neural network.  h ( x , t )  is an auxiliary function, usually designed as zero along the boundary nodes to ensure that the contribution of  N θ ( x , t )  is zero along the boundary, or  h ( x , t ) = t  when serving as embedding initial conditions.   Conservation law enforcement is a hard-constrained neural network technique that incorporates physical conservation principles, such as mass, momentum, or energy conservation, directly into the network architecture [

42

javascript:;

]. Typical implementations include divergence-free constraints for incompressible flows ( ⁠ ∇ ⋅ u = 0 ⁠ ) [

42

javascript:;

] and the integral conservation of quantities [

119

javascript:;

]. Négiar et al. [

120

javascript:;

] introduced an additional layer in the neural network, which computes a linear combination of outputs at different spatial locations. The training is to drive the PDE constraint to be satisfied collectively at all locations, so that the errors at those locations can be reduced compared to the individually constrained PINN model. Min and Azizan [

121

javascript:;

] enforced hard constraints by appending a differentiable projection layer after the network output that maps predictions onto an input-dependent feasible set (e.g., affine/convex). Thus, it enabled unconstrained end-to-end training while guaranteeing constraint satisfaction. Furthermore, Iftakher et al. [

122

javascript:;

] proposed a Karush-Kuhn-Tucker (KKT)-HardNet, which introduces a differentiable projection layer. By solving the KKT system of an orthogonal distance-minimization problem, it projects network outputs onto the feasible set and enforces nonlinear equality and inequality constraints. These methods eliminate the need to include explicit residual terms in the loss function, as the network structure inherently satisfies the governing physical laws. Variational formulations with hard constraints reformulate PDEs as functional optimization problems or their integral equivalents, where the loss is defined based on the weak form of the governing equations. The hp-variational physics-informed neural network framework [

123

javascript:;

] exemplifies this approach by incorporating the PDE residual into the loss through variational integration, thereby enforcing the underlying physics as a hard constraint. A representative loss function in this formulation is given by L = ∫ | R ( y θ ) v | 2 d Ω (14) where  y θ  is the output of the neural network,  v  is the testing function,  Ω  is the solution domain, and  R ( y θ )  is the residual.  

Neural operators also demonstrate strong potential for incorporating hard constraints within their frameworks, aiming to learn solution operators of PDEs that inherently satisfy the governing equations. Hard constraints can be applied to neural operators through mechanisms similar to initial and boundary condition embedding, as well as conservation law enforcement. Representative models such as deep operator networks (DeepONets) [

124

javascript:;

] and the Fourier neural operator (FNO) [

125

javascript:;

] have shown the capability to embed such constraints. However, most existing hard constraint techniques have primarily focused on standard neural networks, leaving neural operators a promising direction for future development.

4.2 Physics-Informed Gaussian Process Regression.

GPR, also known as Kriging, is a nonparametric statistical model that supports active learning, where its model update is based on the Bayesian approach. The core idea of GPR is to model the unknown function  f ( x )  as a Gaussian process (GP), described by  f ( x ) ∼ G P ( m ( x ) , k ( x , x ′ ) ) ⁠ , where  m ( x )  is the mean function of input  x ⁠ , and  k ( x , x ′ )  is the covariance function or kernel function that characterizes the similarity between two inputs  x  and  x ′ ⁠ . In contrast to the common supervised-learning workflow that utilizes a fixed training dataset, GPR enables sequential active learning. That is, the current mean and variance functions are used to choose new query locations via an acquisition criterion (e.g., uncertainty level or information gain). The corresponding new observations are collected, and the model is updated with Bayes’ rule. The assumption of a Gaussian distribution makes each update computationally efficient because of Gaussian–Gaussian conjugacy, while the kernel’s hyperparameters and the noise level are estimated by maximizing the log marginal likelihood or via a fully Bayesian treatment [

126

javascript:;

].

The original GPR is a generic mathematical model. For specific applications, the GPR surrogate model, however, could violate physical laws, and its predictions could be infeasible. For instance, the input–output relationship needs to be monotonic. In this case, monotonicity constraints need to be incorporated into GPRs. Derivatives can be included as a co-kriging approach where both the values and the gradients of the target function are predicted, and the derivatives are then constrained to be only positive or negative [

127

javascript:;

,

128

javascript:;

]. Inequality and convexity constraints can also be incorporated in GPRs using truncated Gaussian distributions so that the posterior predictions are bounded [

129

javascript:;

–

131

javascript:;

]. In this case, Markov chain Monte Carlo sampling is used for Bayesian model update.

By integrating domain-specific physical knowledge, such as PDEs, analytical equations, initial and boundary conditions, into the GPR framework including kernel functions and the type of distributions, the model’s adherence to fundamental physical laws can be significantly enhanced. Classical “physics-in-the-prior” approaches replace or augment the GP mean function with a mechanistic model or design new kernels that encode physical structure. For example, Hassani et al. [

132

javascript:;

] embedded a degradation law in the mean function to achieve accurate fault diagnosis and remaining-useful-life prediction for rolling-element bearings. Similarly, Long et al. [

133

javascript:;

] directly used PDEs as the mean functions to construct GPRs. Zhu et al. [

134

javascript:;

] constrained the GP mean function with a tool-wear model to improve extrapolation stability in manufacturing settings. To predict the acoustic and dynamic behaviors of structures, Jones et al. [

135

javascript:;

] designed a new GPR kernel function based on the Laplacian eigenfunctions. Li et al. [

136

javascript:;

] designed a new kernel with the additive features of hydrodynamic characteristics. Chang and Zeng [

137

javascript:;

] directly used a neural network to learn and customize the kernel function, whereas physics was embedded with the Boltzmann-Gibbs distribution instead of the Gaussian. For solid mechanics problems, Frankel et al. [

138

javascript:;

] embedded the rotational invariance of the stress-stretch constitutive relation in GPR so that the strain energy density can be predicted with fewer samples. Fuhg and Bouklas [

139

javascript:;

] generalized the tensor function representations of stresses to anisotropic materials, and a small number of inducing points were selected to construct local GPRs to improve scalability.

GPR can also be applied as a probabilistic learning approach to solve PDEs, where the likelihood that the residuals are minimized and boundary/initial conditions are satisfied can be evaluated at certain collocation points. This approach can be regarded as operator learning under probabilistic and soft constraints. For example, Raissi et al. [

140

javascript:;

] utilized a GPR model to learn the nonlinear relationship between the spatial locations and the PDE solution, as well as the load vector. Chen et al. [

141

javascript:;

] used the GPR surrogates to learn PDEs where the temporal and spatial derivatives are explicitly mapped to the ones of GPR models. Pförtner et al. [

142

javascript:;

] showed that the GPR-based operator learning can be applied and integrated with collocation, spectral, and Galerkin methods to solve linear PDEs. Hamelijnck et al. [

143

javascript:;

] constructed GPR models to predict both state variables and the derivatives in linear and nonlinear PDEs. Dalton et al. [

144

javascript:;

] further extended this approach by introducing hard constraints of domain boundaries based on R-function so that the solution domain can be further generalized to have arbitrary shapes.

4.3 Physics-Informed Generative Models.

Conventional generative models such as VAE [

145

javascript:;

], generative adversary networks (GANs) [

146

javascript:;

], and diffusion models [

147

javascript:;

] have shown considerable promise in capturing complex data distributions and enabling tasks such as uncertainty quantification, data reconstruction, and synthetic data generation. However, these models often operate as black boxes, lacking adherence to the fundamental physical principles that govern the systems under study. This deficiency can lead to outputs that are statistically plausible yet physically inconsistent, thereby limiting their applicability in engineering and scientific problems where the laws of physics provide essential constraints.

To address this critical limitation, the concept of physics-informed generative modeling has emerged as a powerful paradigm. By embedding physical laws into the structure and training objectives of generative models, these approaches ensure that the generated outputs are not only data-consistent but also physically valid. Among these models, physics-informed variational autoencoders (PI-VAEs), physics-informed generative adversarial networks (PI-GANs), and physics-informed diffusion models (PIDMs) have attracted particular attention due to their complementary strengths and wide-ranging applicability.

4.3.1 Physics-Informed Variational Autoencoder.

In PI-VAEs, prior physical knowledge is embedded directly into the latent variable structure and learning objectives. This integration provides both interpretability and regularization, ensuring that the generative process aligns with established physical principles. The general formulation of PI-VAE augments the traditional evidence lower bound (ELBO) by incorporating a physics-informed residual term as a soft constraint, yielding the training objective as L PI − VAE = E q ϕ ( z ~ | x ) [ log p θ ( x | z ~ ) ] − D KL ( q ϕ ( z ~ | x ) ‖ p ( z ~ ) ) + γ L P (15) where  x  denotes the observed data as input, and  z ~  is the latent variable.  q ϕ ( z ~ | x )  represents the variational posterior parameterized by  ϕ ⁠ .  p θ ( x | z ~ )  is the likelihood function modeled by the decoder. The prior  p ( z ~ )  is typically Gaussian, while  γ  regulates the influence of the physics-informed residual loss  L P ⁠ , which penalizes deviations from known physical laws. This formulation ensures that the model learns latent representations that balance data reconstruction with physical consistency, providing a foundation upon which more sophisticated variants are built.  

Hora et al. [

148

javascript:;

] applied the PI-VAE to reconstruct turbulent wall-bounded flow fields from sparse planar measurements. By incorporating physics-based regularization, their model accurately recovered 3D flow structures, outperforming conventional methods in preserving small-scale turbulence features and statistical accuracy. Kulkarni and Sabato [

149

javascript:;

] employed a similar PI-VAE framework for structural health monitoring, focusing on full-field thermal expansion and damage detection in metallic structures. The model, trained solely on healthy data, effectively identified defects in both simulated and experimental scenarios, demonstrating strong generalization and robustness to the sparsity of the measurements. In the field of industrial fault detection, Lai et al. [

150

javascript:;

] combined physics-based simulations with a PI-VAE to detect anomalies in electro-hydraulic servo actuators used in aircraft engines. Their method outperformed traditional autoencoders and physics-only models, achieving more reliable fault detection with reduced false alarm rates under limited data availability. Zhong and Meidani [

151

javascript:;

] extended the basic PI-VAE to solve forward and inverse problems involving stochastic differential equations (SDEs). Using maximum mean discrepancy for latent space alignment, they demonstrated the model’s ability to efficiently learn complex stochastic dynamics and provide accurate uncertainty quantification.

A notable extension of the basic PI-VAE is the vector quantized PI-VAE (VQ-PI-VAE), which introduces discretization into the latent space through vector quantization. The loss function is augmented as  L VQ − PI − VAE = λ D L D + λ VQ L VQ + λ P L P .  In this expression,  L D  promotes fidelity to the observed data, and  L VQ  enforces closeness to discrete latent codebook entries.  L P  maintains adherence to physical constraints. The coefficients  λ D ⁠ ,  λ V Q ⁠ , and  λ P  balance the influence of each term. This variant has proven effective in applications such as fusion plasma simulations [

152

javascript:;

], where substantial improvements in data compression have been demonstrated while preserving the essential physical characteristics of the system.

Another variant of PI-VAE called a physics-informed dynamical variational autoencoder ( ⁠ Φ -DVAE) model [

153

javascript:;

] addresses the assimilation of high-dimensional and unstructured observations into dynamical systems governed by known physics, while the observation operator and physical parameters remain unknown. The model factorizes the joint distribution of observations, latent embeddings, physical states, and parameters as p ( y 1 : N , x 1 : N , u 1 : N , Λ ) = p ( Λ ) ∏ n = 1 N p ( y n ∣ x n ) p ( x n ∣ u n ) p ( u n ∣ u n − 1 , Λ ) (16)  

Here, the pseudo-observations  x n  act as learned embeddings bridging the raw observations  y n  and the physical latent states  u n ⁠ , which evolve according to known dynamical laws parameterized by  Λ ⁠ . The encoder neural network maps observations to the latent space, while the latent state posterior is approximated using an extended Kalman filter. The physical parameters  Λ  are jointly inferred with the latent states and embeddings.

Training maximizes the ELBO, which balances the reconstruction likelihoods of observations and pseudo-observations, the physical consistency of latent dynamics, and the regularization of variational distributions. This framework is well-suited for applications such as unstructured data assimilation, physical parameter inference, and long-term forecasting in dynamical systems. It has been demonstrated on systems like Lorenz-63 and the Korteweg–de Vries equation, successfully recovering system parameters and latent dynamics from high-dimensional observations, while providing uncertainty quantification and enabling physically consistent extrapolation.

The dual latent variable formulation introduced by Takeishi and Kalousis [

154

javascript:;

] represents a significant advancement in the PI-VAE framework, offering a structured separation between physics-informed and data-driven representations within the latent space. This architecture employs two latent variables:  z P ⁠ , encoding physical parameters, and  z A ⁠ , capturing residual, nonphysical complexities. The priors are specified as  p ( z P ) = N ( m P , v P 2 I )  and  p ( z A ) = N ( 0 , I ) .  The generative process integrates both components as  x = F [ f A , f P ; z P , z A ] + ε .  In this formulation,  f P  represents the deterministic physics model.  f A  is a neural network modeling unstructured component.  F  combines the outputs of these functions, and  ε  accounts for observational noise.

The encoder approximates the posterior factorization as q ψ ( z P , z A | x ) = q ψ ( z A | x ) ⋅ q ψ ( z P | x , z A ) (17)  

The training objective extends the ELBO to include separate Kullback–Leibler (KL) divergences for each latent space.

To preserve the meaningful contribution of the physics model  f P ⁠ , Takeishi and Kalousis [

154

javascript:;

] introduced additional regularizers: the regularization of predictive posterior consistency and two stages of physics encoder alignment (RDA-1 and RDA-2). These regularizations ensure that the neural network component does not overshadow the physics model and that the encoder accurately infers physically consistent latent representations. The method was applied to a range of synthetic and real-world tasks, including pendulum dynamics, advection-diffusion systems, galaxy image generation, and human gait modeling. In these diverse settings, the dual latent PI-VAE demonstrated superior generalization performance, particularly in out-of-distribution scenarios. The model exhibited strong extrapolation capabilities, accurately reconstructing trajectories and physical parameters beyond the training distribution. Moreover, the ability to manipulate the physics-grounded latent variables allowed for controllable and interpretable generation, enabling counterfactual analysis by directly varying physical parameters such as angular velocity or diffusion coefficients. Overall, the dual latent PI-VAE effectively balances physics-based priors and data-driven learning, yielding models that are not only accurate and robust but also capable of providing physical insight into the generative process.

4.3.2 Physics-Informed Generative Adversarial Network.

PI-GANs extend the conventional GAN framework by embedding physical constraints into the adversarial training process. The foundational structure comprises a generator network that synthesizes solutions based on latent inputs and a discriminator network that seeks to distinguish between generated and real data samples. Crucially, PI-GAN integrates governing physical equations directly into the training objective as soft constraints, thereby ensuring that the generated solutions not only align with observed data but also respect the underlying physical laws of the system.

The standard formulation of PI-GAN augments the Wasserstein GAN with a gradient penalty objective to promote stable training. The generator loss is given by L G = − E z ~ ∼ P z ~ [ D ( G ( z ~ ) ) ] (18) where  G ( z ~ )  represents the generator output for a latent variable  z ~ ⁠ , and  D ( ⋅ )  is the discriminator output. The discriminator loss is defined as L D = E z ~ ∼ P z ~ [ D ( G ( z ~ ) ) ] − E x ∼ P data [ D ( x ) ] + λ E x ^ ∼ P x ^ ( ‖ ∇ x ^ D ( x ^ ) ‖ 2 − 1 ) 2 (19) where  λ  regulates the strength of the gradient penalty, enforcing the Lipschitz constraint critical for stable GAN training. To incorporate physics, PI-GAN introduces an additional penalty term that penalizes violations of the governing physical laws, such as L P = 1 N ∑ i = 1 N ‖ N x [ u ( x i ; G ( z ) ) ] − f ( x i ) ‖ 2 (20) where  N x  represents the differential operator of the governing equation, and  f ( x i )  is the known source term or forcing function at point  x i ⁠ .  

PI-GAN has been applied across a range of complex physical systems. Its effectiveness has been demonstrated in solving high-dimensional SDEs [

155

javascript:;

–

157

javascript:;

] and PDEs [

158

javascript:;

]. By enforcing stochastic PDE constraints within the GAN framework, the model accurately learned solution manifolds and their associated uncertainties, even in the presence of sparse or noisy data. Compared to PINNs, PI-GAN exhibited superior stability in approximating stochastic solutions due to its adversarial training mechanism.

In the domain of computational mechanics, PI-GAN has been effectively deployed for material property identification. Warner et al. [

159

javascript:;

] utilized PI-GAN to infer spatially varying elasticity fields from limited displacement observations. The integration of elasticity PDE constraints ensured that the predicted material fields were both data-consistent and physically plausible, enabling accurate inverse reconstruction of heterogeneous materials.

Further extending its application, He et al. [

160

javascript:;

] employed PI-GAN for multiaxial fatigue life prediction in engineering structures. Their framework incorporated well-established fatigue models within the GAN training objective, enabling the generator to produce life predictions that adhered to empirical fatigue criteria while capturing data-driven uncertainties. The resulting model outperformed conventional empirical approaches, offering improved accuracy in fatigue life estimation under complex loading conditions.

In fluid dynamics, PI-GAN has been adopted for multiphase [

161

javascript:;

] and turbulence super-resolution tasks. Subramaniam et al. [

162

javascript:;

] demonstrated that PI-GAN could generate high-fidelity, high-resolution turbulent flow fields from coarse input data by embedding the Navier-Stokes equations into the training objective. This approach effectively captured fine-scale turbulence structures and improved energy spectrum recovery compared to baseline super-resolution methods.

Collectively, these studies underscore the versatility and effectiveness of PI-GAN in learning physically consistent generative models across a diverse set of applications. The integration of physics constraints within the adversarial training paradigm enables PI-GAN to maintain data fidelity while ensuring compliance with governing equations, leading to robust performance even in settings characterized by data sparsity, high dimensionality, and complex physical behaviors.

4.3.3 Physics-Informed Diffusion Model.

Diffusion models, rooted in a probabilistic framework, were originally developed for image generation. They have shown remarkable success in capturing complex data distributions through purely data-driven training [

147

javascript:;

]. Although this approach ensures that the generated samples are statistically consistent with the training data, it does not guarantee compliance with the underlying physical principles [

163

javascript:;

,

164

javascript:;

]. Therefore, PIDMs were developed to extend traditional denoising diffusion models by incorporating physical constraints into the training process.

The theoretical basis of the diffusion model comes from the nonequilibrium thermodynamics and the probabilistic process [

147

javascript:;

,

165

javascript:;

]. Classic diffusion models, such as denoising diffusion probabilistic models (DDPMs), can gradually transform the original data distribution into a standard normal distribution. It is achieved by constructing a forward diffusion process that gradually adds Gaussian noise to the data in the form of a Markov chain. Then, neural network models, such as U-Net, are employed to explore the hierarchical structures of noisy data and generate new clean samples that statistically resemble the original data. At their core, diffusion models operate through a forward process that gradually adds noise to data, and a reverse process that learns to denoise. The forward process progressively adds noise to the data  x 0  until  x T ∼ N ( 0 , I ) ⁠ . This process follows the Markov chain that transforms a data sample  x 0 ∼ q ( x 0 )  into a set of noised samples  x t  in  T  steps by q ( x t ∣ x t − 1 ) = N ( x t ∣ α t x t − 1 , ( 1 − α t ) I ) (21) where  α t  is a noise schedule controlling the gradual corruption of data, and  I  is the identity matrix ensuring isotropic noise. The noise schedule is usually predefined to ensure a smooth transition from data to pure noise over  T  steps. The reverse process aims to gradually remove noise from a purely noised data  x T  to recover a sample that has the same distribution as  x 0 ⁠ . This process is achieved by a conditional probability distribution  p θ ( x t − 1 ∣ x t ) ⁠ , which is parameterized by neural networks. The training of the neural network is performed by optimizing the usual variational bound on negative log-likelihood. In actual training, the diffusion model usually employs this approach by L = E x 0 , ε * , t [ ‖ ε * − ε θ ( x t , t ) ‖ 2 ] (22) where  ε *  is random noise following a normal distribution  N ( 0 , I ) ⁠ , and  ε θ  is a function approximator intended to predict  ε *  from  x t ⁠ .  

PIDMs combine the generative capabilities of diffusion models with the physical constraints of PINNs, aiming to overcome the limitations of both approaches. Bastek et al. [

163

javascript:;

] introduced a method for integrating physical laws by regularizing the loss function. Specifically, the physical constraint is expressed as a residual operator  R ( x 0 ) = 0 ⁠ , which is incorporated as an additional loss term. Thus, alongside optimizing the standard diffusion model objective  E x 0 ∼ q ( x 0 ) [ log p θ ( x 0 ) ] ⁠ , a physical constraint term  E r ^ [ log p θ ( r ^ ) ]  is also optimized, where  r ^  represents the residual of the governing equation  R ( x 0 ) = 0 ⁠ . Another prominent approach to incorporate physical laws into diffusion models is through conditional diffusion models, which was proposed by Shu et al. [

166

javascript:;

]. This method incorporates physical laws as conditional information within the DDPM framework. The idea can be expressed as  p θ ( x 0 : T ) = p ( x T ) ∏ t = 1 T p θ ( x t − 1 ∣ x t , ∂ r t ∂ x ) ⁠ , where  ∂ r t ∂ x  serves as the physics-informed condition. In this formulation, the guidance is provided by a learned combination of the residual gradient and noise in the DDPM. This approach has proven effective in high-fidelity computational fluid dynamics data reconstruction from low-fidelity inputs. However, these methods are typically limited to static 2D PDEs and cannot handle spatiotemporal dynamics.

To address this limitation, Pi-fusion [

167

javascript:;

] was proposed to incorporate PDE loss into the overall loss function using denoising diffusion implicit models [

168

javascript:;

]. This integration brings temporal information into the training process. Pi-fusion is capable of solving spatiotemporal Navier–Stokes equations. In contrast to Pi-fusion’s approach, the projected generative diffusion models (PGDMs) [

169

javascript:;

] enforced physics-informed output generation during the sampling process via a projector-guided operation. This ensures that the generated samples satisfy a set of physical constraints. Similarly, Jacobsen et al. [

164

javascript:;

] integrated physical laws during the sampling stage and adopted a physically consistent sampling method to further minimize the PDE residual of the generated output. Table 

2

javascript:;

 provides an overview of these PIDMs.
