4.4 Operator Learning and Symbolic Regression

4.4.1 Physics-Informed Operator Learning.

Operator learning, such as DeepONet [

170

javascript:;

] and FNO [

125

javascript:;

], aims to learn the mapping (i.e., an operator) from one function space to another. It leverages data-driven methods, particularly neural networks, to approximate operators and address high-dimensional problems. Physics-informed operator learning combines neural operators with physical constraints to achieve better performance compared to PINNs or data-driven operator learning alone. The integration of operator learning and PINN has proven to be highly effective not only in solving forward and inverse problems of PDEs [

171

javascript:;

,

172

javascript:;

] but also in the simulations of fluid dynamics, heat transfer, and other scenarios [

173

javascript:;

–

175

javascript:;

].

One of the representative operator learning algorithms is DeepONet [

170

javascript:;

], which decomposes an operator into two components: feature extraction from the input function and basis function representation at spatial points. It employs a branch network to encode input function features and a trunk network to learn the basis functions, with the final output obtained via their inner product. This decomposition significantly reduces the complexity and computational cost of operator mapping. FNO [

125

javascript:;

] is another very effective operator learning framework. It uses the Fourier transform to map functions into the frequency domain, where operator mapping is learned. The architecture of FNO includes a lifting layer, multiple Fourier layers, and a projection layer. The lifting and projection layers are used for the linear transform, while the Fourier layers are used to extract the refined features. Moreover, there are other operator learning algorithms, such as neural operator [

176

javascript:;

], learning operators with coupled attention [

177

javascript:;

], and graph neural operator [

178

javascript:;

]. These operator learning algorithms demonstrate strong efficiency and generalizability. However, they are highly data-dependent, computationally expensive, and often lack physical consistency because of the absence of embedded physics information.

A key focus in operator learning is to embed physics so that the models remain physically consistent. Traditionally, this is achieved by augmenting the training objective with PDE residuals and boundary/initial conditions (in strong or weak form), enabling optimization in low- or no-label regimes and improving out-of-distribution extrapolation. Representative frameworks include physics-informed neural operator [

175

javascript:;

], physics-informed DeepONet [

179

javascript:;

], multifidelity and Bayesian operators [

180

javascript:;

], and finite operator learning [

181

javascript:;

]. More recently, hard enforcement of physics at the architectural/discretization level has gained traction. Structure-preserving operator networks [

182

javascript:;

] were developed to integrate operator networks within a finite element discretization to preserve key properties of the continuous system and robustly handle complex geometries and boundary conditions. Conservation-preserved FNO [

183

javascript:;

] introduced a learnable correction/projection that maps network outputs onto the solution subspace satisfying specified conservation laws, achieving exact conservation without relying on soft penalties in the loss function. Complementing these, Harandi et al. [

184

javascript:;

] proposed a label-free spectral approach that enforces mechanical equilibrium in Fourier space via a Lippmann–Schwinger formulation, reducing AD overhead and improving speed/accuracy for heterogeneous materials. Finally, Boya and Subramani [

185

javascript:;

] presented a physics-informed transformer neural operator. By stacking iterative integral-operator blocks with cross-attention, it learns condition-aware representations that generalize effectively to unseen initial/boundary conditions, achieving low errors on fluid dynamics PDE benchmarks.

4.4.2 Physics-Informed Symbolic Regression.

Physics-informed symbolic regression (PISR) is an extension of conventional symbolic regression (SR), which aims to automatically derive analytical expressions from observed data that capture the relationships between input and output variables [

186

javascript:;

]. By incorporating physical prior knowledge into the symbolic regression process, PISR enhances model interpretability and ensures consistency with known physical laws [

9

javascript:;

,

187

javascript:;

]. Its primary objective is to uncover underlying physical principles in a symbolic form, even in the presence of noisy or sparse data.

Unlike traditional regression methods, such as linear regression and polynomial regression, SR does not presuppose a specific form of a model, but rather searches the space of mathematical expressions to find the best symbolic function. The output is usually an interpretable analytical expression rather than a numerical prediction or black-box mapping. Given a dataset  { ( x i , y i ) } i = 1 N ⁠ , SR is able to find a function  f ( x )  that achieves  f ( x i )  to approximate  y i  as much as possible, while keeping the expression simple and generalizable at the same time. The optimization objective for SR is based on minimizing the data fitting error. The complexity penalties such as expression length or number of operators are included to avoid overfitting [

188

javascript:;

]. The expression of SR can be written as f * = arg min f ∈ F * ∑ i = 1 N ( y i − f ( x i ) ) 2 (23) where  x i  represents the input data.  y i  is the corresponding output.  f ( ⋅ )  signifies the mathematical expression to be learned from the function space  F * ⁠ .  f *  indicates the optimal expression that minimizes the error.  

By incorporating physical knowledge into the SR process, PISR is developed to uncover underlying physical principles in a symbolic form [

9

javascript:;

,

187

javascript:;

]. Compared to traditional SR, PISR shrinks the hypothesis space and thus tends to return simpler, more explainable expressions with improved data efficiency [

189

javascript:;

]. A key approach in this area is sparse identification of nonlinear dynamics (SINDy), which fits a sparse set of terms from a dictionary to recover governing ODEs [

190

javascript:;

]. Subsequent variants have extended this concept to PDE discovery, often referred to as PDE functional identification of nonlinear dynamics or PDE-SINDy [

19

javascript:;

]. Additionally, Deep-SINDy [

191

javascript:;

] combines SINDy with learned coordinates via autoencoders, while weak-form SINDy adopts weak-form testing to enhance robustness under noisy and irregular sampling [

192

javascript:;

]. In contrast to sparsity approaches, artificial intelligence (AI)-Feynman fuses dimensional consistency, functional separability, and symmetry with a small neural network to prune the search space before symbolic solving, which lowers computational cost and improves out-of-distribution fidelity [

9

javascript:;

].

PISR has demonstrated significant advantages across various applications. A recently developed open-source computational framework called scientist-machine equation detector demonstrated a modular “scientist-in-the-loop” pipeline. It allows for components, such as prior filters, simulator checks, the SR core, and validation, to be easily adjusted. With stronger priors, it achieves high efficiency and accurately recovers physical laws from challenging sedimentation experiments that involve nonlinear drag, noise, and missing features [

193

javascript:;

]. In engineering domains, PISR has been adapted to model the tool-wear process with domain constraints [

194

javascript:;

] and analyze nonlinear wave dynamics using physics-guided bases and weak residuals [

195

javascript:;

]. For materials screening, the sure-independence screening and sparsifying operator offer a PISR-style compressed sensing framework. This framework discovers low-dimensional, physically consistent descriptors for phase discrimination and alloy design, enabling the creation of interpretable maps for rapid screening [

196

javascript:;

].

4.5 Physics-Informed Graph Neural Networks.

GNNs are a class of deep learning models that naturally capture the topological or spatial relationships between elements. In various engineering applications, the elements and relationships are usually modeled as nodes and edges of graphs, respectively, such as networks, power grids, supply chains, mesh models, grain structures, etc. The learning of GNNs involves node update and message passing between nodes. Particularly, the learning of convolutional GNNs has information propagation through local neighbors in the spatial domain (e.g., GraphSAGE [

197

javascript:;

], graph attention networks [

198

javascript:;

], and heterogeneous graph neural network (HetGNN) [

199

javascript:;

]) or spectral domain after graph Fourier transform (e.g., graph convolutional network (GCN) [

200

javascript:;

] and hypergraph neural network (HGNN) [

201

javascript:;

]). Special GNNs have been developed to accelerate engineering simulations, such as solving the Poisson equation [

202

javascript:;

], performing super-resolution [

203

javascript:;

], simulating mesh deformation and adaptive meshing [

204

javascript:;

], geometry evolution [

205

javascript:;

], and multifidelity graphs [

206

javascript:;

].

Physical and domain-specific information can also be embedded in GNNs to improve their performance. Similar to PINNs, the residual in the weak formulation of PDEs can be directly utilized in the loss function of GNNs [

106

javascript:;

]. Other physical information including energy, boundary conditions, and initial conditions can also be embedded in the loss function to guide the learning process [

102

javascript:;

,

107

javascript:;

,

207

javascript:;

]. For complex problems such as simulating continuum deformable body, both spatial and temporal correlations need to be considered. Additional information can be modeled as hidden states with temporal prediction approaches such as RNNs to predict the message passing and edge update process of GNNs [

208

javascript:;

]. In combination with reinforcement learning (RL), GNNs can be applied to model the network of agents [

209

javascript:;

]. GNNs have also been utilized to predict structural failures. Failure and fragment modes can be embedded in GNNs. New active learning strategies can also be introduced in the message passing so that the probability of failures can be predicted [

210

javascript:;

,

211

javascript:;

].

4.6 Physics-Informed Reinforcement Learning.

RL aims to learn the optimal policy to maximize cumulative reward through interactions between an agent and the environment. Unlike traditional supervised and unsupervised learning methods that update models based on training data, RL optimizes a policy depending on trials and errors. Specifically, the agent selects actions according to the states of the environment and its policy. These actions will affect the environment, leading to state changes and rewards. In turn, the agent updates its policy to improve future responses.

To enhance the physical plausibility, effectiveness, and learning efficiency of RL for complex problem solving in the real world, physics-informed reinforcement learning techniques have been developed. Due to the unique structure and learning strategy of RL, the methods of embedding physics into the RL architecture are slightly different from those of other ML techniques. The key elements of the RL model, including state, action, reward and penalty functions, and policy and value networks, can all be modified to incorporate physical knowledge. Thus, the following subsections will detail how the physics information is embedded in these elements.

4.6.1 State Design.

The environment states in RL can be more constructive for agents by considering physical knowledge. There are generally three types of methods for physics-based state design. The first method aims to combine the states of other agents with those of the target agent using the fusion strategy inspired by prior knowledge, as illustrated in Fig. 

12(

a

)

. The second method involves fusing additional physical information with the states of the target agent. The additional information can be either known physical knowledge or calculated states based on prior knowledge, as demonstrated in Fig. 

12(

a

)

. The last approach is to add physical constraints to the state space for selecting the specified states, which is presented in Fig. 

12(

c

)

. The added physical constraints can be either conservative rules or the representation network that generates states in accordance with the system characteristic.

Fig. 12

View large

Physics-based state design methods: ( ⁠ a ⁠ ) state fusion with other agents, ( ⁠ b ⁠ ) state fusion with physical information, and ( ⁠ c ⁠ ) physics-constrained state selection

Fig. 12

View large

Download slide

Physics-based state design methods: ( ⁠ a ⁠ ) state fusion with other agents, ( ⁠ b ⁠ ) state fusion with physical information, and ( ⁠ c ⁠ ) physics-constrained state selection

Close modal

State fusion with other agents and additional information has been applied to achieve car-following control for connected automated vehicles (CAVs) [

212

javascript:;

]. The real-time motion of each CAV was influenced by motion information from downstream vehicles, roadway geometry, and ground-truth vehicle trajectories. The multipredecessor information was weighted summed according to equilibrium and consensus concepts from the control theory. This scheme achieved great lateral control accuracy and stable oscillation damping performance of CAVs. Similarly, for aircraft conflict resolution, Zhao and Liu [

213

javascript:;

] encoded real observations, such as aircraft’s position, speed, and heading angle, and imaginary data generated based on physical prior into image representations to search for the optimal policy. The imaginary data include physics modeling of the prediction and conflict detection using a safety bound model. In adaptive cruise control, Jurj et al. [

214

javascript:;

] designed an additional state called the minimum jam-avoiding distance according to the agent states and prior knowledge of avoiding collisions. With this strategy, the cruise shows fewer collisions and more equidistant travel than the results of using traditional RL.

The approach of adding state constraints has been applied in the formation control of mobile robots, where a barrier function was designed to restrict the state information that can only be perceived from neighbor agents [

215

javascript:;

]. The barrier function was incorporated into the policy, which effectively enables collision avoidance control for robots. Additionally, in voltage control of robust distribution systems, Cao et al. [

216

javascript:;

] embedded the structural correlations of measurement data by forming graph-structured data into a graph attention network to imitate the calculation process of power flow. The representation network can fill missing values or smooth noisy measurements using information from adjacent nodes and extract informative features for decision-making. Therefore, this approach improves the robustness of voltage control against anomalous measurements.

4.6.2 Action Management.

The agent’s actions should obey the safety protocols of the physical environment so that RL can be truly implemented in the real world. An effective method to ensure safe actions is to apply shields [

217

javascript:;

] or filters to check the rationality of actions. The shields are usually integrated with simple and conservative rules derived from practices or expert knowledge [

218

javascript:;

]. If the actions violate operation constraints, the shield can refine the actions to be appropriate. The rationality of an action can be evaluated based on the action itself or the state it produces. Additionally, physical knowledge can be reflected in the design principle of shields, safety protocols, and the process of modifying unsafe actions into safe ones.

Post hoc shielding is the most common action management method, which tries to check agent actions before applying them to the environment. Post hoc shielding has been widely used in complex operating conditions that require continuous action output, such as various energy control. For example, Chen et al. [

219

javascript:;

] developed a physics-based shielding mechanism to avoid excessive charging or discharging in safe voltage control of power distribution systems. If unsafe states occur, the shielding mechanism can replace all related actions with safe ones inferred according to the physical and electrical characteristics of the battery energy system. Meanwhile, it imposes a penalty on the agent to promote safe policies. In the cooling control of buildings, Wang et al. [

218

javascript:;

] designed a post hoc rectification, based on the thermodynamics governing the heat process, to rectify unsafe RL recommendations. An LSTM network, trained by fitting historical safe operation traces, was used to capture the thermodynamic laws and extrapolate the transitions to unsafe states. Similarly, Yu et al. [

220

javascript:;

] utilized a barrier function to adjust the power consumption of the cooling system based on the comfort constraints of the building temperature. Postposed shielding is also very critical for the control of robots and automotive vehicles. Feng et al. [

221

javascript:;

] leveraged a safety layer to achieve multi-agent collision avoidance in the robotic system. The modification operation in the safety layer is a constrained optimization problem that aims to find the action closest to the identified unsafe one while satisfying the constraint of safety distance. This strategy has also been utilized in automotive vehicle control [

222

javascript:;

,

223

javascript:;

], where the safety constraints were defined according to driving dynamics, traffic rules, and physical priors, such as speed and distance restrictions, as well as the control limit from vehicle theory.

Pre hoc shielding, also called preemptive shielding [

224

javascript:;

], is another shielding method that removes unsafe actions before training the agent. It is usually applied in scenarios that require discrete action outputs. Fisac et al. [

225

javascript:;

] developed a preemptive shielding framework by integrating Hamilton–Jacobi reachability methods from control theory with Bayesian analysis based on empirical observations. It guarantees that the search space of the learning algorithm satisfies the specified constraints by exploiting approximate knowledge of system dynamics. This method successfully enabled the safe flight of a quadrotor vehicle under strong external disturbances. Zhang et al. [

226

javascript:;

] employed preemptive shielding for energy management of hybrid electric vehicles, where a rule-based coach is designed to ensure safety training. The coach checks whether the calculated engine speed and torque can ensure that each power component in the power train satisfies the physical limits. If not, the coach generates new control signals using the rule-based energy management strategy.

Although shielding-based methods are easy to implement and have ensured the safety outputs of RL in many applications, they often lead to suboptimal policies. This is because the conservative laws incorporated may interfere with the training process [

227

javascript:;

]. One way to overcome this is to avoid the infeasibility of actions by constraining the exploration process during agent training. Inspired by this concept, Biswas et al. [

228

javascript:;

] developed a physics-informed exploration (PIE) strategy for safety energy management of hybrid electric vehicles. If infeasible actions occur, the PIE strategy chooses new actions from the predefined safety action set while updating the bounds of the safety action set according to the physical cause of infeasibility from prior experience. Otherwise, the actions will be directly calculated by a policy network embedded with prioritized experience replay (PER), which prioritizes transitions of action-state pairs from previous training. The coupling of PIE and PER improves adherence to safety constraints and reduces overall violations during training. She et al. [

229

javascript:;

] achieved safety training by mapping the output of the actor network into real-time gains using a transformation rule derived from the numerical model of the control system. This action mapping ensures that the control gains remain in a rational range defined by engineering practices, thereby helping microgrids achieve accurate trajectory tracking.

4.6.3 Reward and Penalty Design.

Physical task-oriented rewards can endow interpretability to the agent’s strategy [

230

javascript:;

] and help assign more accurate rewards for agent actions [

231

javascript:;

]. The related techniques usually modify reward functions according to physical objective functions or constraints to force agents to operate following specified laws. The general form of the physical reward function in most literature can be roughly expressed as (there may be slight differences in different application scenarios) r t = ζ M ( H ( a t ) − H g ) + η w ( a t ) (24) where  H ( a t )  and  H g  mean the effect caused by the action  a t  and the target effect at time  t ⁠ , respectively.  M ( ⋅ )  is the function that calculates the partial reward value based on the difference between the real and target effects.  w ( a t )  is another type of effect caused by  a t ⁠ .  ζ  and  η  are the weights. Hence, this reward function guides the agent to take actions toward approaching the target effect while satisfying certain performance criteria.  

The design of physical task-oriented rewards has successfully facilitated RL in tackling complex problems across various fields. For instance, in optimizing the nuclear fuel assembly, Radaideh et al. [

232

javascript:;

] shaped the reward function according to the physical rules followed by nuclear engineers. The agents are guided to position fuel rods in the assembly while adhering to constraints on the rod number and position, as well as an energy optimization strategy. This enables high-dimensional assembly while ensuring safety and economic viability. Li et al. [

233

javascript:;

] proposed a federated multi-agent RL algorithm for effective and efficient energy management of a microgrid system. The reward function was designed to achieve two physical targets: training the agent while meeting the requirements of operational cost and self-energy sufficiency. In the application of fluid fields, Dang and Ishii [

231

javascript:;

] designed a compound reward by integrating the prior theory of interfacial area (IA) transport into a Markov process to predict IA of two-phase flow. The reward function was constructed in the form of prediction error related to change trend, value precision, and inertia by integrating the physical models, experimental observations, and the inertia change of IA concentration. Yousif et al. [

234

javascript:;

] constructed a multi-agent RL model to reconstruct flow fields from noisy data, with each agent corresponding to one pixel and actions focused on selecting optimal filters. A physics-based immediate reward was formulated by combining the momentum and pressure Poisson equations with boundary conditions that represent the reconstruction error of velocity. The reward function directly mimics the denoising process of flow velocity, allowing the model to follow an optimal denoising strategy.

Physical task-oriented reward design has also been applied to promote various motion control. In the automatic operation control of the tunnel boring machine (TBM), Lin et al. [

235

javascript:;

] proposed a physical reward function derived by weighted summing the advance speed and the pressure difference between the chamber pressure and the earth pressure. This reward function allows simultaneous evaluation of both the efficiency and stability of excavation progress. A similar method has been used in Ref. [

236

javascript:;

] for the energy-efficient operation of a TBM working on soft ground. The reward function was designed to gain marks from high advance speed and lose marks from consumption of kinetic inertia energy. In a reference-guided RL technique for generating complex character animation, Peng et al. [

237

javascript:;

] defined the reward based on the characteristics of the reference motion to encourage the character to imitate known movements.

Like the design of physical task-oriented reward, penalties can also be defined in terms of physical constraints. In this way, penalties can be imposed if the exploration process violates physical constraints. This idea has been adopted by Zhang et al. [

238

javascript:;

] to optimize the charging strategy of electric vehicles. They formulated the charging scheduling problem as a constraint Markov decision process to find an optimal policy to maximize the operator’s profit. The optimization process was constrained by the cumulative penalty derived by considering the limit of the power capacity and the power balance requirements of the energy system, as well as the constraints of demand satisfaction.

4.6.4 Policy and Value Function Design.

An excellent policy in RL helps guide the agent in making optimal decisions according to environmental states, thereby maximizing long-term rewards and speeding up convergence. Incorporating physical knowledge of the environment into policy design is an effective way to enhance the policy, since it can ensure that the generated actions adhere to environmental constraints.

Since policy is generally a mapping network from environment states to agent actions, most existing methods for designing physics-based policy utilize techniques such as PINN, PDE, and hybrid models. These works will not be introduced in this section. A unique technique for embedding physics into the policy architecture is presented in Ref.[

239

javascript:;

], which combines FNN with a power flow network to solve the optimal power flow (OPF) problem of the renewable power system. The framework of this method is illustrated in Fig. 

13

javascript:;

. The FNN predicts voltage parameters, which are then input into the power flow network to compute operational parameters. The power flow network was built based on power balance equations to ensure that actions meet the constraints of the system. Apart from actions, the policy network generates all decision variables essential for calculating the cost function according to the inequality constraints of the operational limits. The policy network was optimized by propagating constrained gradients and integrating the policy gradient with the cost function gradient. The designed physical policy can automatically correct infeasible actions, thereby satisfying all the constraints for OPF problems.

Fig. 13

View large

Download slide

Physics-informed policy

Fig. 13

View large

Download slide

Physics-informed policy

Close modal

Incorporating physical knowledge into the value function can enhance policy training and reduce computational burden. A physics-guided value function can be constructed using analytically derived physical heuristic functions. This technique has been demonstrated in Ref. [

240

javascript:;

], where the value function was designed to be a flow-control-informed function based on the self-organizing criticality formulation applied to an idealized Batchelor-Kraichnan model. The proposed scheme was applied to address the challenge of controlling a swimmer’s separation from a passive target in chaotic flows, which is difficult to achieve with traditional RL methods.

4.7 Physics-Constrained Dictionary Learning.

Different from general ML models that learn a surrogate model to represent the complex relationships between inputs and outputs or extract patterns from input data, dictionary learning seeks to learn an optimal dictionary that can represent dense signals by a linear combination of the dictionary and a sparse coefficient vector [

241

javascript:;

]. Incorporating physical knowledge during dictionary update allows the designed dictionary to have high representation ability even with defective input data and data acquisition restrictions in practical applications. On the other hand, considering physical constraints related to data acquisition can also facilitate the optimization of the data acquisition strategy using dictionary learning. This is because dictionary optimization algorithms endeavor to construct an optimal dictionary using the most critical and representative data points. This is valuable for improving data acquisition efficiency. For instance, when an entire temperature field needs to be reconstructed with sparse data points, prioritizing more measurements in regions exhibiting high temperature gradients enables capturing intricate temperature details. Conversely, areas with low temperature gradients necessitate fewer measurements. Moreover, when reconstructing the complete measurement field using limited data points, it is crucial to factor in sensor specifications such as size, resolution, sampling rate, and sensitivity for optimal results.

To integrate sensor placement strategy optimization into the monitoring of manufacturing processes, Lu and Wang [

242

javascript:;

] developed a physics-constrained dictionary learning (PCDL) method to incorporate sensor specifications as constraints when a measurement matrix  Φ ⁠ , a basis matrix  Ψ ⁠ , and a classification matrix  C  are optimized simultaneously. It is to solve min Φ , Ψ , C α ‖ S − Ψ ξ ‖ F 2 + ‖ Φ S − Φ Ψ ξ ‖ F 2 + β ‖ L − C ξ ‖ F 2 (25) s . t . Φ = f ( Ψ ) (26) ‖ ξ i ‖ 0 ≤ s l ∀ i (27) ℷ ( Φ ) ∈ P (28) where  S  and  L  represent training data and class labels, respectively.  ξ  represents the sparse vectors of coefficients for the original data.  α  and  β  denote the weights associated with reconstruction and classification errors, respectively.  f ( Ψ )  refers to the method, such as a constrained FrameSense algorithm [

242

javascript:;

], used to optimize the measurement matrix when the basis matrix is provided.  s l  is the sparsity constraint applied to the sparse coefficient vectors.  ℷ ( Φ )  indicates the column indices of the nonzero values in each row of the measurement matrix  Φ  which corresponds to the locations of the measured data points. Lastly,  P  is the search space, which is constrained by various sensing strategies. The framework for simultaneously optimizing the measurement, basis, and classification matrices is shown in Fig. 

14

javascript:;

. With the training data and initial basis matrix  Ψ 0  as the input, the constrained FrameSense algorithm was applied to optimize the measurement matrix  Φ  by incorporating physical constraints related to the sensing strategies [

243

javascript:;

]. For instance, when the single-probe sensing strategy is used to collect point-wise measurements, the minimum pair-wise distance between the measurement locations is limited by a threshold value. Thus, the redundant information included in the adjacent measurements is reduced. With the optimized measurement matrix and the initial classification matrix  C 0 ⁠ , K-means singular value decomposition (K-SVD) was performed to obtain the updated basis and classification matrices. These processes are repeated until the results are converged. With the optimized matrices, the original high-resolution data can be obtained as  Ψ ξ ⁠ , where  γ  is recovered by an orthogonal matching pursuit algorithm. The machine states can also be identified as  C γ ⁠ .

PCDL has also been applied to quantify porosity distribution in laser powder bed fusion of copper [

244

javascript:;

] and classify the machine conditions in the fused filament fabrication process measured by vibration signals [

245

javascript:;

,

246

javascript:;

]. With less redundant information in the sparse coefficient vector, both the classification accuracy and efficiency can be improved. To monitor the powder spattering in the laser powder bed fusion process with optical images, additional features such as edges in the images were used to further enhance the classification accuracy [

243

javascript:;

]. The results using the extended feature-based physics-constrained active dictionary learning (FB-PCADL) method are shown in Fig. 

15

javascript:;

. PCDL has also been adapted to combine vibration signals from multiple sensors to detect bearing faults in rotating machinery [

247

javascript:;

]. In this application, the measurement, basis, and classification matrices for each sensor were optimized individually. Subsequently, the classification matrices from all sensors were fused together, resulting in improved fault identification with higher classification accuracy compared to using a single sensor. With the optimized sampling rate, the rotating machinery condition can be monitored with much less data without sacrificing the collected information.

While the previous discussion primarily focuses on integrating physical knowledge into the sampling strategy in PCDL, the physical knowledge can also be effectively used to construct the basis matrix. This approach can enhance the sparsity of the original data following transformation in a specific physical domain, allowing for the elimination of most redundant information during data processing. Furthermore, the basis matrix can be extended to higher dimensions to capture more correlations among high-dimensional data.

4.8 Physics-Based Compressive Sensing.

While many PIML methods primarily emphasize the forward prediction of the complete physical field with simulated or experimental data, the integration of physical insights into data-driven models also enables the use of measured data to extrapolate unobservable information.

Compressed sensing is known as a pure data-driven approach to improve data acquisition efficiency by taking advantage of the sparsity representation of signals after predefined transformations such as the Fourier transformation and the wavelet transformation. To further enhance the compression ratio and reconstruction accuracy of traditional compressed sensing, Lu and Wang developed a physics-based compressive sensing (PBCS) method to reconstruct the complete measurement field based on limited measurements by incorporating the knowledge of physical phenomena in specific applications [

248

javascript:;

,

249

javascript:;

]. The general procedures of PBCS consist of two main steps, as shown in Fig. 

16

javascript:;

. In the recovery step, an inverse problem is addressed to recover the load vector  ξ  within the discretized physical model, utilizing sensor measurements  y *  typically gathered at the boundaries of the domain. The reconstruction step then involves solving the forward model to predict the complete distribution of the physical quantity as the latent field. This approach allows PBCS to seamlessly integrate modeling in the digital space with sensing in the physical space. Essentially, the inverse problem in the recovery step in PBCS is to solve min ‖ y * − Φ y ‖ 2 (29) s . t . y = f ( t , ξ , d , x ˙ , △ d , … ) (30) ‖ ξ ‖ 0 < s l (31) where  f ( t , ξ , d , x ˙ , △ d , … )  describes the relationships among physical quantities  d  as well as their time and spatial derivatives  ( d , x ˙ , △ d , … ) ⁠ . Typically, this function represents physical models, which are often formulated as PDEs. The minimization process can be based on the criteria of  l 2  norm. There are numerous algorithms available for recovering the load vector  ξ ⁠ . These include basis pursuit [

250

javascript:;

], least absolute shrinkage and selection operator (LASSO) [

251

javascript:;

], least angle regressions (LARS) [

252

javascript:;

], nuclear norm minimization [

253

javascript:;

], matching pursuit [

254

javascript:;

], orthogonal matching pursuit [

255

javascript:;

], CoSaMP [

256

javascript:;

], Fourier sampling algorithm [

257

javascript:;

], focal underdetermined system solver (FOCUSS) [

258

javascript:;

], and others.

View large

Close modal

PBCS has been applied to reconstruct the 3D temperature field of the printed part in the fused filament fabrication process. With a small set of temperature measurements on the printed part’s surface, the boundary conditions, including heat flux, convection, and radiation, represented as a condensed load vector of the heat transfer model, were recovered with a high compression ratio compared to the traditional compressed sensing. After that, the complete 3D temperature field can be reconstructed by the forward modeling. Due to enhanced data acquisition efficiency, PBCS has facilitated the development of digital twins for monitoring the fused filament fabrication process, enabling seamless integration between physical experimentation and digital modeling [

259

javascript:;

]. The results in monitoring the real-time temperature field in fused filament fabrication are shown in Fig. 

17

javascript:;

. A similar concept has been extended to reconstruct the complete velocity field within a wind tunnel by capturing a sparse set of point-wise flow velocities near the tunnel boundaries [

260

javascript:;

]. The detailed recirculating flow over a backward-facing step was also captured. PBCS has also been applied to monitor the melt pool within the laser powder bed fusion process by incorporating a multiphysics model for the thermal-fluid field. PBCS demonstrated the capability to reconstruct both the temperature and fluid fields of the melt pool solely through surface temperature measurements [

261

javascript:;

]. In addition to process monitoring, PBCS has also been extended to inversely identify internal heat sources based on the surface temperature measurements [

262

javascript:;

,

263

javascript:;

].

Close modal

javascript:;

PBCS can be regarded as a sensing algorithm well-suited for handling real-time and streaming data, as it operates without the need for model training. This characteristic makes PBCS a highly robust approach for enhancing sensing efficiency, allowing for the accurate reconstruction of complete physical fields using only a limited number of measurements and the reliable prediction of hidden or unmeasured physical quantities. In addition to the applications mentioned above, PBCS also has significant potential in various scenarios. For instance, PBCS can be applied to detect internal cracks or stress concentrations in bridges, buildings, or aircraft using limited surface strain or vibration data. In reconstructing pollution or temperature fields in the atmosphere, PBCS can be integrated with sparse sensor networks for real-time environmental assessment. In battery or electrochemical systems, the internal state of batteries can be reconstructed through limited surface measurements for predictive maintenance.

4.9 Other Generic Techniques for Neural Network.

Apart from the unique techniques of embedding physics into specific ML models described in the previous sections, there are also some generic techniques suitable for all neural network models. These techniques try to assign physical meaning to the parameters, components, and neurons of neural networks, which will be detailed in the following subsections.

4.9.1 Physics-Guided Parameter Initialization.

Many neural network models need to initialize parameters before training, such as weights, biases, and learning rates. Most existing techniques choose to randomly initialize these parameters, which may result in poor convergence and getting stuck in local optima of the model. Physical knowledge-guided parameter initialization is a promising way to solve this problem, as the physics-specified parameters are more closely aligned with the underlying structure of the model and data [

264

javascript:;

].

A common approach for physics-guided parameter initialization is to use transfer learning. Specifically, the ML model is first pretrained on a related task prior to obtain the suitable parameters, which are then transferred to construct the target task model. Many works have shown to pretrain neural network models using synthetic data from simulation models. Aliakbar et al. [

265

javascript:;

] utilized this technique to initialize PINN for solving multiphysics PDEs, where the low-fidelity data from the simulation of computational fluid dynamics were used for pretraining. This approach helps to obtain reliable results without high-fidelity training data. Similarly, with the two-step multifidelity training, Tang et al. [

266

javascript:;

] successfully predicted the time-domain response of optical resonances and inferred the corresponding resonance frequencies in optical science. Read et al. [

267

javascript:;

] initialized the LSTM network to predict lake water temperatures using predictions from a 1D lake hydrodynamic model [

268

javascript:;

], which tracks state variables with Lagrangian layers. They also demonstrated that simulation-based neural network pretraining adapts better to unseen scenarios compared to purely simulation models. Park et al. [

269

javascript:;

] reported to incorporate the adjoint gradient into RL by initialization for designing highly complex optical devices. They pretrained the agent network using the figure-of-merit gradient estimated from two electromagnetic simulations, which significantly enhanced the sampling efficiency for structure optimization. Rodwell and Tallapragada [

270

javascript:;

] utilized a pretraining method to overcome the difficulty of controlling fish-like swimming robots with complex unmodeled physics. They first trained the agent network to perform increasingly complex tasks in a representative nonholonomic system to capture some primary physics. Then, the agent network was transferred to a small amount of high-fidelity data from the fluid-robot interaction system.

Using simulation models for parameter initialization is also an effective way to address the difficulties of neural networks in acquiring large amounts of costly data. However, effective initialization depends on a high-fidelity simulation model, which increases demands on both modeling complexity and computational costs. Another parameter initialization method for neural networks is to leverage numerical solutions of system-related process equations or transforms. For example, Tarbiyat and Nemati Saray [

271

javascript:;

] introduced to initialize the weights of PINN with approximate solutions of PDEs. They discretized the related differential operators and obtained an approximate solution using FDM. The initialized weights reduced the training loss of the PINN and improved the accuracy of PDE solving. He et al. [

272

javascript:;

] devised a weight initialization method based on physics-informed wavelet transform in fault diagnosis of rolling bearings. They modified the wavelet basis functions by introducing a scale smoothing factor to form a signal processing-consistent wavelet dictionary, which was then applied to initialize the weights of the first convolutional layer. This technique improved the extrapolation ability of the deep neural network model. Wang et al. [

273

javascript:;

] utilized the least squares solution, which fits the initial condition, to initialize the last layer of the physics-informed residual adaptive network (PirateNet). The adaptive residual connection in PirateNet allows the output of the network to be a linear combination of the first layer embeddings at the initial stage. Therefore, the initialized network can be progressively deepened during training. This initialization strategy enabled the encoding of appropriate inductive biases corresponding to a given PDE system within the network architecture.

4.9.2 Physical Intermediate Variable.

One of the reasons for lacking interpretability in standard neural networks is that they can only extract abstract hidden variables. By introducing physical intermediate variables, certain neurons can be assigned a physical meaning, which helps to extract physically relevant representations. This technique has been applied in the voltage control of the active distribution network [

274

javascript:;

]. The researchers set the voltage out of control ratio as a target estimation variable in LSTM by adding an auxiliary-task head with the form of self-supervised learning. With this physical indicator, the hidden layers needed for training are dramatically reduced.

Another related technique is to assign some weights in neural networks with physically meaningful values and keep them unchanged during training. This approach has been applied to develop a theory-guided RNN for the modeling of seismic waveform inversion [

275

javascript:;

]. Most parameters in the RNN were forced to mimic the wave propagation process and fixed with values obtained from governing equations. The only trainable parameter is the wave velocity, which is the induced intermediate variable related to the process. The researchers demonstrated that this technique yields more robust training compared to simply introducing intermediate variables.

4.9.3 Physics-Informed Component.

Improving the interpretability of neural network models can also be achieved by modifying some components in the architecture to enable them to achieve physics-related tasks. One typical example is to replace the convolutional kernels of CNN with trainable wavelets [

276

javascript:;

], which facilitates the extraction of frequency information from the input time series. Similarly, Hao et al. [

277

javascript:;

] employed a temporal causal convolution layer as the input layer of the transformer, which helps the model learn the local context of the input data. Additionally, the mathematical equation representing cutting physical knowledge was embedded in the output layer as a hard constraint. Experiments showed that the proposed method significantly improved prediction accuracy and the robustness of the transformer. In robotic control, Bahl et al. [

278

javascript:;

] employed the dynamical structure of the system, which is described by the second-order dynamic movement primitives equation of trajectory distribution, as a layer of the deep policy network. The parameters of the dynamical system were predicted as outputs of the preceding layers. The embedded structure enables the agent to explore in the space of physically plausible trajectory distributions.

In addition to layers, the activation functions in neural networks can also be assigned with a physical meaning. Howland and Dabiri [

279

javascript:;

] formulaically modified the sigmoid activation function to fit a thrust coefficient curve in the modeling of wind farm power output. The modified activation function can predict downstream power from upstream power, facilitating the capture of nonlinear fluidic wake interactions between successive layers of turbines. Wang and Huang [

280

javascript:;

] applied Softmax and absolute activation functions to the output layer of the actor network in RL, which maps the output coefficients to ensure compliance with stringent constraints derived from the Minkowski–Weyl theorem. This adaptation helps prevent noncompliant evaluations in the kinematics optimization of a self-propelled articulated swimmer.

5 Challenges and Opportunities of PIML

It is seen that a diverse range of PIML techniques emerged in recent years, particularly various approaches for physics embedding, knowledge embodiment and representation, and information integration. The advancement of PIML has resulted in some new approaches that can be potentially applied to design and manufacturing. However, several open issues remain unsolved, which pose critical obstacles to apply the PIML techniques to solve industry-scale problems. This section summarizes the challenges that PIML faces in the aspects of real-world applications and highlights the potential opportunities to pave the way for future research.

5.1 Challenges.

Despite the advancement of PIML research in the past three decades, several significant challenges still hinder its adoption in industry-scale applications. These challenges can be categorized into several aspects, including training convergence, representation and integration of complex physical models, data imperfection, model generalizability, computational efficiency, model interpretability and reliability, and physics-data conflict.

5.1.1 Training Convergence.

One of the primary challenges in PIML is how to achieve effective training convergence. Integrating physical knowledge into ML models enhances the interpretability and facilitates guided learning, which can potentially improve prediction performance. However, this incorporation of physical knowledge also introduces additional complexities and uncertainties that affect the convergence performance of model training, particularly in multiphysics and high-dimensional scenarios. The physical models that need to be tailored for specific applications can be quite complex. For instance, monitoring the melt pool states in the laser powder bed fusion process requires consideration of factors such as melt flow, temperature fields, recoil pressure, and surface tension [

261

javascript:;

]. Training a model that captures both the statistical correlations in the data and the underlying physical principles requires a significant number of iterations, and the results can easily diverge. Additionally, using a PIML model to predict physical quantities in complex scenarios necessitates a considerable number of hyperparameters, which further increases computational challenges.

Another major challenge affecting convergence is the uncertainty in the data and physical models. When the input data for the PIML model comes from experimental measurements, uncertainties such as calibration errors, instrument bias, and environmental factors are common. In such cases, the measured data may not adhere precisely to the underlying physical principles. Consequently, this can hinder the convergence of the PIML model. On the other hand, uncertainty in physical models is also inevitable, especially when the understanding of the true underlying physics is limited. All models involve simplification and abstraction, and therefore do not reflect the true physical phenomena either. Thus, the mismatch between physical models and data increases the difficulty of training.

5.1.2 Representation of Complex Physical Phenomena With Incomplete Knowledge.

The representation and integration of complex physical phenomena also pose significant obstacles for PIML in real-world applications. Most of the existing PIML research efforts focus only on single-physics models. Single-physics modeling relies on assumptions and boundary conditions so that the intricately coupled multiphysics systems in real-world scenarios are simplified and the physical quantities are assumed to be independent from each other. Nevertheless, comprehensive multiscale multiphysics modeling is still necessary if we would like to obtain an accurate and detailed description of physics in modern engineering products or complex processes in advanced manufacturing. For example, the metal additive manufacturing process involves metal melting and solidification, fluid flow, heat conduction, and oxidation processes [

281

javascript:;

]. When constructing the process–structure–property relationship of metal additive manufacturing with PIML models, thermomechanical behavior, fluid dynamics, and phase change phenomena should be comprehensively considered. Relying only on simplified and approximated single-physics models and isolated knowledge may result in incoherent predictions of physical processes, thus failing to fully leverage the advantages of integrating physical knowledge in ML to effectively solve real-world problems. Constructing multiphysics models that accurately simulate complex physical phenomena is already very challenging, which requires researchers to have comprehensive knowledge and sufficient experience. Further integration with ML as in PIML undoubtedly poses great challenges.

Even with detailed dependency relationships introduced in multiphysics and nonlinear models, human knowledge about complex phenomena is still incomplete. For example, turbulent flow is characterized by high instability, featuring vorticity, diffusion, and energy dissipation processes. However, existing turbulence models still cannot capture the turbulence in atomic superfluids. There are still no comprehensive theories that consistently describe the complex phenomena [

282

javascript:;

]. Integrating a physics-based model in PIML to describe the superfluid turbulence will introduce errors and discrepancies. Thus, physics-based modeling can still face the knowledge gap and limitations. The gap and limit inevitably lead to uncertainty, which will be inherited by PIML models.

5.1.3 Data Imperfection.

Similar to traditional ML, data are essential for PIML models to learn complex relationships and explore patterns of the system. The quality of data directly influences the reliability of PIML predictions as well as the training efficiency. Data scarcity is a common problem in engineering applications of ML. Data scarcity is caused by several factors, including the system complexity which requires a high-fidelity description, thus more data to capture the nonlinear input–output relationships, high costs associated with data acquisition, especially in application areas where high-throughput experimental data collection methods are not available, as well as the situation that data availability is restricted due to proprietary concerns or regulatory constraints. Because of the insufficient training data for PIML to fully explore and capture complex input–output relationships, data scarcity may also hinder the generalization of PIML to unseen scenarios [

283

javascript:;

,

284

javascript:;

].

Data scarcity is also related to data imbalance caused by the scarcity of certain classes of data. In applications such as fault and anomaly detection in manufacturing, certain states or phenomena of interest occur infrequently compared to others. For example, defects of debris, soot, and misprint in additively manufactured products rarely appear compared to normal regions [

285

javascript:;

]. Acquiring data under those rare or extreme conditions is also costly. For example, expensive computed tomography tools are necessary to detect microscopic pores in metal parts. To train ML models for classification problems, balanced amounts of training data for different classes are important for classification accuracy. Data imbalance may affect the prediction accuracy of PIML models for classification [

18

javascript:;

].

Another aspect of the data imperfection problem is the poor quality of training data. Data collected from experiments can be noisy, redundant, and incomplete, caused by systematic and random errors of the instrument, environmental disturbances, equipment failures, or running out of time or resources. Low-quality data not only provide limited useful information for PIML models but also distort the learning process [

156

javascript:;

]. Noisy training data can force models to replicate noise, instead of real input–output relations. If the distributions of noise differ between the training dataset and the validation dataset, the validation loss can increase. Furthermore, incomplete data may lose the key features of complete data, which can make the results of PIML models difficult to understand and interpret.

5.1.4 Generalizability.

The generalization ability of PIML models is essential to their application to real-world problems. However, PIML models often suffer from limited generalizability. When geometric variation, changes of input parameters, and diversity of physical systems occur, a PIML model that is trained with data from one scenario cannot be easily reused for another.

First, when PIML is trained on a fixed geometry, it may not generalize well to new domains with different shapes or topologies. This is due to the fact that PDE solutions are highly sensitive to the geometry of the solution domain and the boundary conditions [

286

javascript:;

]. Some approaches have been proposed to address this problem, such as solution domain decomposition [

287

javascript:;

] and neural operators [

125

javascript:;

]. These methods can learn geometry-invariant representations in theory. However, in practice, the accuracy of approximations in these approaches still depends on the levels of nonlinearity or discontinuity in different application scenarios. Secondly, most of the PIML models rely on fixed input parameters such as material properties, source terms, or boundary values. However, in many engineering and scientific applications, input parameters vary significantly across different problem instances. The ability of a model to generalize across such variations is critical for its practical applicability. Methods such as parametric PINNs [

288

javascript:;

] incorporate parameters directly into the input space, enabling the model to learn a family of solutions. Moreover, operator-learning-based approaches, such as DeepONets and FNOs, are specifically designed to learn mappings from input functions or parameter fields to the distribution of solutions. However, they still cannot bypass the limitation of extrapolation inherent in ML. Finally, the PIML models are usually designed for a specific type of PDEs, ODEs, or algebraic equations. The diversity of physics exists in nature, and the physical models have a wide variety of forms, which differ in their mathematical structures. Moreover, different boundary and initial conditions can also drastically alter the solution space. Addressing this problem is challenging. Incorporating the flexible inductive biases or learning the symbolic representations of models can be helpful. However, it is extremely challenging, if not impossible, that a generic and universal PIML model is constructed by learning the physics of different domains, under the limits of model complexity and available training data, and at the same time can be specialized in solving domain-specific problems with very accurate predictions.

5.1.5 Computational Efficiency.

Adding physical knowledge to ML models will increase the computational complexity of the training process, since it is similar to adding constraints in optimization or adding more criteria and restrictions in reasoning and decision-making. PIML models must strike a balance between fitting the training data and staying adherent to physical laws, which is particularly evident in the training of PINN models [

289

javascript:;

]. For PIML models other than PINNs, even though physical knowledge is not incorporated into ML through a physical loss, it can still expand and increase the parameter space that needs to be searched through during learning. These additional parameters correspond to the physical properties. When physical principles such as stress-strain relationships, dynamic stability criteria, or phase transition of materials are introduced in PIML models, they have influences on the model training so that the predictions are tailored toward the specific physical properties. This expansion of the parameter space complicates the training process and increases the required number of iterations.

Furthermore, when physical models are embedded into neural networks, the computational complexity of AD to compute derivatives increases. This process can generate additional large tensors during backpropagation, which can impose a significant burden on the transmission and storage of these tensors. Moreover, AD-based back-propagation involves long computational chains in which errors accumulate over multiple layers. The additional gradients of physical models may increase the accumulated error and impair training stability for fast convergence. Some researchers developed numerical approaches for discretization such as finite difference, finite element, and finite volume methods to calculate the embedded physical models without AD involved. However, the discretization approaches also require computational resources for mesh generation, refinement, and other additional procedures.

Computational efficiency issues are also prevalent when simulations are used to generate synthetic data for training or as inputs in the hybrid models. Especially when high-fidelity simulations are required to ensure the reliability of the synthetic data, such as finer meshes needed to discretize the computational domain, they inevitably lead to high computational costs. Parallel computation can potentially be used to speed up the training of some PIML models. Nevertheless, the added physical constraints may complicate this process. This is because the parallelization may sacrifice the coherence of physical laws in a distributed computing environment. Therefore, the computational efficiency issue must be considered when applying PIML techniques. The issue cannot simply be assumed avoidable using more computational resources. Researchers need to holistically consider how to reduce the model complexity and quantify uncertainty when incorporating physical knowledge into ML.

5.1.6 Interpretability and Extrapolation Capability.

Interpretability refers to the ability of a model to provide transparent and explainable insights about the model’s construction, its predictions, or causal relationship revelation. The interpretability of ML models is crucial for users to assess the reliability and suitability of the results obtained for specific applications [

290

javascript:;

]. Some methods and tools have been developed to interpret the predictions of ML models, such as local interpretable model-agnostic explanation (LIME) [

291

javascript:;

], Shapley additive explanations [

292

javascript:;

], and Captum [

293

javascript:;

]. Yet, they can only achieve the basic level of sensitivity analysis for different inputs or factors. Despite the original intention of embedding physics in PIML models is to provide explainable predictions, the interpretability of most existing PIML models is still very limited. This is because the current techniques of embedding domain knowledge in ML models are still largely based on the original black-box ML approaches, such as modifying the loss function or employing hybrid models. There are no modifications to the fundamental architecture of the ML models. As a result, the domain-specific physics information can easily get diluted and lost within the ML architecture during information propagation. Furthermore, the complexity of advanced architectures for deep learning makes it difficult to explain the process of how the ML structures and internal components were selected. Deep learning models are inherently difficult to interpret.

Another reason for the interpretability issue is that the current research priority for PIML has been on the incorporation of physical and domain knowledge to improve the prediction accuracy of pure data-driven models, instead of the improved model construction process to avoid the loss of domain knowledge in the ML models. The focus of the latter is important to enhance the interpretability of PIML models. This may require the modification of ML architectures for reliable and tractable representations of physical knowledge and more transparent data-physics integration.

It had been hoped that embedding physical and domain knowledge in ML models could improve not only interpretability, but also extrapolation capability. Yet, PIML models still suffer from the same extrapolation issue as the data-driven ML models. Early studies [

11

javascript:;

] have shown that this challenge is particularly predominant in the PINN models. The root causes of the extrapolation issue in PIML models are the techniques of embedding physics, similar to the interpretability issue. The mixture of losses affects the training convergence, which can lead to the violation of physical laws. Although operator learning is intended to learn general functional mappings, the reliance on the classical ML architectures makes those models inherit the undesirable ML limitations including extrapolation, in spite of some recent research efforts [

294

javascript:;

–

297

javascript:;

] that aim to enhance the temporal extrapolation capability of neural operators.

5.1.7 Physics-Data Conflicts.

The data collected from experiments and simulations may contradict established physical models or principles. Physical models are constructed with necessary approximations and simplifications, whereas experimental techniques contain systematic and random errors. It is not uncommon that discrepancy exists between data and models. When training PIML models to achieve conflicting goals, it is difficult to make the models converge toward any of them. This will lead to poor convergence and inaccurate predictions. In PINNs, the convergence speeds for the reduction of losses are also sensitive to the relative weights assigned to different loss terms.

In addition, data collected from experiments may contain physical quantities of interest that are different from the ones that are directly included in the known physical laws or models. The integration of them in one PIML model would require us to establish the relation between them. When conversions between physical quantities are complex, the mathematical relations between them are often developed empirically. For instance, the density of point defects in metallic materials due to radiation can only be observed indirectly, usually by measuring the electrical resistance changes and inferring through some empirically derived relationship. In those cases, the integration of physical models and experimental measurements in PIML models can be challenging.

5.2 Applications in Industry.

Although researchers have applied PIML techniques to solve engineering problems, their adoption in industry remains limited. Some attempts and case studies were made to apply PIML for problems in the manufacturing industry. For instance, in automotive production, physical-loss-based PINN models can be applied to predict thermal power in air handling units for topcoat painting and to forecast temperature profiles in degreasing tanks [

298

javascript:;

], and predict surface roughness in milling operations [

299

javascript:;

]. PINNs also have the potential to predict vibration and wave propagation in composite materials for the aerospace industry [

300

javascript:;

]. Furthermore, the hybrid model technique has been applied to predict the product completion time in production systems [

301

javascript:;

]. The physics-embedded architecture technique has been utilized to diagnose the health of aerospace structures [

302

javascript:;

], and to improve the metal removal rate in electrical discharge machining [

303

javascript:;

].

Solving industry-scale problems requires reliable, robust, and scalable PIML tools. Complex systems in industry involve interacting subsystems. Phenomena and factors at multiple length and time scales need to be considered. For example, in the aerospace industry, the effects of fluid-structure interactions, thermomechanical behaviors, structural fatigue, and corrosion need to be considered simultaneously in designing aircraft. The structural reliability, stability, and maneuverability of the aircraft need to be optimized together. To accurately describe the involved physics, engineers are required to have an interdisciplinary and deep understanding of the problems when building models for multiphysics multiscale simulations. The construction and execution of such complex physics-based models are time- and resource-consuming. Although PIML models offer cost-effective alternatives, the challenges discussed in Sec. 

5.1

https://asmedigitalcollection.asme.org#s5a

 have been the major barriers to make the PIML a practical tool for industry applications. In addition, industry-scale models often operate in a dynamic environment. Assumptions made in academic settings cannot always be satisfied. For instance, when ML models are trained on a fixed dataset before deployment, it is assumed that the operating conditions do not change. Yet, models that present the best prediction performance only for a specified operating condition will not be useful for industry applications. As a result, it is challenging to develop a PIML model that is sophisticated enough to adapt to the diverse conditions and ever-changing processes in industries.

Therefore, the immaturity of the PIML techniques limits their applications in industry. At the same time, industry practitioners may be hesitant to adopt these new technologies due to the concerns about not only reliability, interpretability, and robustness, but also the potential disruption of the established business processes.

5.3 Opportunities.

Although PIML raised significant interest in engineering research communities, the fundamental challenge of its prediction errors for large-scale problems remains. Its applicability in real-world engineering problems is still unknown. Nevertheless, this fundamental challenge offers good research opportunities for researchers at the same time. Several areas of research efforts are needed.

First, the training and learning algorithms need to be significantly improved for better convergence. The high dimensionality of the parameter space is the root cause. Efficient global optimization algorithms will allow the training to converge to global minima of losses. The complexity of training is particularly prominent for large-scale problems where multiphysics models are involved. To minimize several loss terms simultaneously, especially when these losses are intrinsically coupled and correlated, is a daunting task. There is a large gap between the needs in real-world applications and the current available global optimization algorithms. Novel methods and approaches for large-scale multi-objective optimization will alleviate the bottleneck in PIML. Furthermore, novel approaches for PIML with hard constraints are needed to tackle the challenges of training convergence, multiphysics, and extrapolation capability.

Second, the quality and quantity of the training dataset affect the training results. Research issues of imbalanced datasets for classification problems, as well as missing data, noisy data, and bias in general affect the model predictions. New approaches to mitigate the negative effects of these issues. For instance, resampling and generative approaches can be applied to alleviate the effects of these training data issues. More cost-effective utilization of training data, such as multifidelity ML models, also helps construct more reliable PIML models. In addition, active learning that cost-effectively acquires new data points for training and model updates is necessary for real-world applications. The model can be trained with the representative set of scenarios and updated when or where the physics of the problem exhibits significant variation. An effective sampling strategy can help mitigate issues such as gradient imbalance and uneven distribution of training data, which often hinder model convergence and accuracy. By prioritizing samples that capture critical features of the underlying physics, such as areas of high gradient, the model can learn more effectively and make more accurate predictions.

Third, quantifying the uncertainty in PIML models is an important research area that can improve the accuracy and trustworthiness of predictions. The majority of uncertainty quantification methods [

304

javascript:;

] are data-driven by nature. Many are also regarded as statistical ML methods, such as Bayesian inference in Bayesian networks, Markov chains in diffusion models, stochastic differential equations for generative models, Gaussian process in active learning, dimensionality reduction techniques in principal component analysis and manifold learning, and compressive sensing in dictionary learning. Uncertainty in PIML can be introduced from either data-driven or physics-based side of modeling. Model-form uncertainty and parameter uncertainty in PIML need to be differentiated so that they can be managed accordingly. Incorporating surrogate-based models such as Bayesian neural networks and Gaussian process can enhance the prediction capabilities of PIML and alleviate the physics-data conflicts. Dimensionality reduction can help tackle the challenge of computational efficiency in training.

Fourth, reduced-order modeling can improve the performance of PIML models. One of the ultimate goals for PIML is to speed up the predictions in comparison with traditional physics-based modeling for large-scale problems. The complexities of PIML models for these problems will be high, as complex multiscale multiphysics models are involved. Thus, methodologies for reduced-order modeling are helpful to reduce the computational complexities. The integration of ML into reduced-order modeling can achieve an acceleration of orders of magnitude faster in high-fidelity simulation [

305

javascript:;

]. Similar strategies can be taken to construct PIML models.

Finally, more effective mathematical representation methods can be developed for PIML models. Most physical models in the existing PIML techniques are static and single-physics focused, with relatively simple structures. Researchers often prefer utilizing well-established and genetic representations to creating tailored models for specific problems. Enhancing the representations to capture detailed physical processes can not only unlock the full potential of PIML but also enable the integration of multiscale and multiphysics factors. Adopting a multiscale modeling strategy that encompasses multiple time and length scales can improve both efficiency and utility of PIML models. For instance, graph theory can be used to capture the correlations between atoms in molecules, between defects in material models, between nodes in continuum mechanics models, or between subsystems in complex systems of systems. Drift-diffusion models capture the dynamics of temperature, fluids, defect densities, energy transport, spiral waves and vortices, and statistical descriptors. Furthermore, hybrid approaches [

306

javascript:;

] that integrate the learning at symbolic and syntax levels with the one at the instance level with numerical values can potentially enhance the capabilities of PIML to address some of the aforementioned challenges. The generalizability, adaptability, and flexibility of PIML models can be significantly improved.

6 Conclusion

PIML or scientific machine learning is a new research field that aims to integrate physical and domain knowledge into traditional “black-box” ML models so that the reliability, interpretability, and applicability of ML models for engineering and scientific applications can be improved. The incorporation of physics-based prior knowledge into data-driven ML models is to create more accurate and consistent representations of physical systems, thereby enhancing the training efficiency and robustness of predictions. These hybrid modeling approaches provide a promising avenue for tackling intractable problems in engineering such as design and manufacturing. This article provides a review of the current state of PIML techniques, with a focus on the methodologies of physical knowledge integration and the remaining technical challenges. The knowledge integration methodologies are stratified into three categories: hybrid models, physical-loss-based models, and physics-embedded architectures. Despite the development in the last three decades, PIML still faces major challenges of accuracy and reliability that prevent its adoption by industry. The main conclusions drawn from this review are summarized as follows.

Hybrid model techniques combine the predictions of ML and physical models in parallel or serial, with the aim of leveraging the strengths of both to improve the final prediction accuracy. Physics-based feature engineering is the most representative technique in this category, which generates physical features based on analytical models, numerical simulations, as well as feature processing and extraction approaches. Physics-based feature engineering is an effective way to overcome the difficulty of acquiring large amounts of data required by ML models.

Physical-loss-based models aim to construct a composite loss function of the neural network through automatic differentiation, numerical discretization of PDEs, and adding regularization terms. To solve computation and accuracy problems induced by the physical loss, some advanced techniques, such as a weighting strategy, a gradient improvement method, and a physics-constrained Bayesian neural network, have been developed. Physics-based loss function contributes to guiding the model training process toward adherence to physical laws.

Physics-embedded architecture is the primary technique for improving the transparency of ML, which fuses physical knowledge into the main structures of ML models. Different ML architectures have been employed in the physics embedding techniques. The typical techniques include the design of hybrid ML structures, applying physical constraints in training components, and incorporating functional components and physical variables. The essence of this category is to assign physical meanings to the individual components of ML models.

PIML still faces several major challenges in solving real-world problems, including the difficulties of training convergence and computational efficiency, how to represent and integrate physical knowledge of multiple domains, how to achieve higher transparency in model construction, and how to enhance interpretability and extrapolation capability. These challenges may inspire and drive us into new research directions to further advance PIML techniques.
